Problem Domain
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4
Reduction of Game Size
One of the major problems with the game based solution is the exponential
explosion of states which make up the full game graph, the result being that
complex scenarios will take very long to solve, and worst case result in the com-
puter running out of memory. The LiuSmolka algorithm which serves as the
basis of the solution, has linear time and space complexity relative to the depen-
dency graph in which a ﬁxed-point assignment is to be found. The dependency
graph will be linear in size relative to the game graph, which worst case is ex-
ponential in size relative to the railway network. As such the worst case time
complexity of the solution is still exponential relative to the railway network.
In order to allow for more complex networks to be solved, the most important
point of optimisation is reducing the size of the game graph which needs to be
explored. There are two general approaches which can be taken. The optimal
approach would be to identify states, which are guaranteed to be 0 in the ﬁxed-
point assignment, and ﬁlter them out before they are explored.
The other
approach is to identify heuristics which could be used to prioritise the states.
This could allow for faster discovery of a solution, meaning the exploration of
the remaining pending states is not needed.
==PAGE:54==
42
Reduction of Game Size
4.1
Only Consider Relevant Signals and Junc-
tions
The game graph is comprised of two diﬀerent types of vertices Controller and
Conductor vertices, which represent who made the move which resulted in the
vertex. Of these two types Controller vertices can safely be removed. This is
because Controller vertices are disjunctive, while Conductor vertices are con-
junctive. As such all Conductor successors to a Controller must be veriﬁed to
be winning, before the Controller vertex can be considered winning. On the
other hand only a single Controller successor needs to be found, for a Conduc-
tor vertex to be considered winning.
In the basic game graph, all possible successor Controller states are constructed.
The rules of the game however oﬀers a way to prune some of these Controller
states. The game-rules states, that only a single train can move at a time, and a
moving train will only move to the next linear segment in the current direction,
based on the state of relevant junctions and signals. Given that only a single
train can move at a time, a Controller state can be constructed which attempts
to only allow a single train to move. The result is a potentially large decrease
in Controller successor state being generated for each Conductor state. In the
full game graph the number of Controller states being generated per Conductor
state is equal to all combinations of possible signal and junction conﬁgurations,
meaning 2|J| · 2|S|. After the described pruning the number of successor states
will be variable, but worst case be equal to the total number of possible train
destinations based on the current train positions. Given that it is not possible
to link junctions after each other without a linear segment between, the worst
case number of successors becomes 2|T| meaning the number of trains times 2,
as each train may need to enter a junction from the stem point, resulting in 2
possible destinations for each train.
The Controller successor states are derived by ﬁrst setting all signals and
switches to a default state.
For signals this is red, for junctions it is down.
For each train, simulate moving the train forward until a linear segment has
been met. During this simulation, if a signal is encountered turn it green. If
a junctions is meet where the train does not enter from the stem direction, set
the junction such that the train can pass. If the train enters from the stem
point, create one state for each of the two possible junction conﬁgurations. This
results in one Controller state for every possible train destination. It should be
noted that this approach does not guarantee that only the expected train can
move in the resulting states, as several trains may be in a position where no
==PAGE:55==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
43
signal or junction is between the trains current position and the next.
The graph is reduced such, that for every Conductor state GSConductor, the
minimal set of Controller successors GSConductor
succ
are derived, such that the set of
all possible Conductor successors GSController
succ
of GSConductor
succ
, is equal to the set
produced via the naive approach. This is under the assumption that Conductor
states are considered isomorphic, if all train positions are identical. As such the
ﬁnal result remains unaltered. Meaning if there exists a winning strategy in the
full game graph, then there still exists a winning strategy. Inversely if a winning
strategy did not exists before, then a winning strategy does still not exist. A
winning strategy found in the altered version would also be a winning strategy
in the un-altered version. This technique is based on the similar work described
in [KHV16].
4.2
Filtering & Prioritising States
The technique presented in section 4.1 describes a way in which only relevant
successor states are constructed, reducing the size of the game graph. Another
technique is the usage of lazy evaluation, this does not reduce the game size,
but it does allow the evaluation of states to be deferred. In order to further
reduce size of the explored game, two new concepts are introduced: State ﬁlters
and state priorities.
A ﬁlter is a function which receives a state, and determines if it should be ﬁltered
out. In this case a state GSn being ﬁltered out means it has been determined
that F G
min(GSn) = 0 through means other than the LiuSmolka based solver.
As such there is no need to further explore this state or its successors. The
term ﬁlter could indicate that a state is simply removed. In this case it is an
umbrella term for stopping further exploration of that state. There are several
ways ﬁltering can be done.
The most natural approach is to apply ﬁlters to Controller states. The rules
state that if σ(GSn) = Conductor, then it holds for all gs ∈GSn
succ that
σ(gs) = Controller.
In order to ﬁnd a winning strategy for GSn a winning
strategy needs to be found for at least one state in GSn
succ. This means that it
is safe to remove states from GSn
succ without altering the ﬁnal solution. Remov-
ing Conductor states is dangerous. In order for a Controller state to be winning,
then every successor needs to be winning. This means that removing Conductor
==PAGE:56==
44
Reduction of Game Size
states could result in a Controller state being considered winning, because the
failing states are removed. The result could be a false positive solution.
A Controller state is failing if any of its successor states are failing. As such
a Controller state can be ﬁltered if this can be determined before running the
game solver.
A Controller state encodes all current train positions as swell
as the conﬁguration of all signals and junctions. Based on this the successor
function can be used to simulate all possible train movements. As such if any of
these train movements result in a failing state, then then the current Controller
state is failing. As such a sub-ﬁlter is introduced. This sub-ﬁlter receives a
Controller state, a train and a train destination.
This information as such
forms the deﬁnition of a new Conductor state. The main ﬁlter is thus derived,
by simulating all possible train movements and invoking the sub-ﬁlter on these.
The result of the ﬁlter is thus the conjunction of the sub-ﬁlters, meaning if single
possible successor is failing then the Controller state is failing. This approach
means that ﬁltering a Conductor state has a time complexity of O(|T|) assuming
the sub-ﬁlter has a constant time complexity.
The second technique is to apply ﬁlters on Conductor states. A problem how-
ever arises as described earlier, it does not make sense to remove a Conductor
state. Because of the conjunctive nature of Controller states, removing a failing
successor could mean that a false positive is created, meaning an incorrect solu-
tion is found. As such care needs to be taken when ﬁltering Conductor states.
There are two ways this can be achieved without introducing errors.
The ﬁrst way is to iterate through all Conductor siblings immediately and apply
the ﬁlter. If a single Conductor successor is to be ﬁltered out, then it can be
predetermined that there is no need to explore any of its sibling states. This
means that the successor list can be made empty. This however requires a spe-
cial rule which states that if a Controller state GSControllern does not have any
successors then F G
min(GSControllern) = 0. Normally no successor would result in
an empty conjunction meaning normally F G
min(GSControllern) would trivially be
1. This is however already the case as described in section 3.2. Another concern
would be that all Conductor successors are evaluated immediately, reducing the
usefulness of lazy evaluation. If a Conductor state is ﬁltered, then it and all its
siblings can be forgotten before being remembered by LiuSmolka, meaning the
reduced laziness is not a problem. If no state is ﬁltered and all successors are
determined to be 1 in the ﬁxed-point by the game solver, then they were bound
to be evaluated anyway, meaning it is not a problem. The reduced usefulness
arises if no successor are ﬁltered, but one of them is determined to be 0 in the
ﬁxed-point assignment through the game solver. The result would be evaluated
states being preserved in memory un-needed.
==PAGE:57==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
45
The second approach which could be taken when ﬁltering Conductor states,
would be to add a special variable to game states. This variable could be a
Boolean specifying is a state is trivially 0 in the ﬁxed-point assignment, as such
not producing any successors when asked. This approach has the advantage
that the lazy evaluation remains intact. The possible downside is that there is
a risk of exploring the successors of some Conductor sibling states before the
trivially 0 one is encountered.
In this project only Controller states are ﬁltered as described, by applying sub-
ﬁlters to all successor deﬁnitions. The time complexity is linear in the number
of trains which is acceptable, and does not reduce laziness. This approach as
such negates the primary risk associated with both ways of applying ﬁlters to
Conductor states.
The other concept is called a priority. A priority is quite similar to a sub-ﬁlter,
receiving the same input. Instead of resulting in a Boolean, a priority should
result in a value whose type forms a total order. This value can then be used
to sort sibling states. The purpose of prioritising states is to guide the game
solver to search certain parts of the graph before others. This could serve two
purposes. One is to use the prioritisation as a heuristic, guiding the search such
that fewer states are explored. This usage has some overlap with ﬁlters, but
because states are not removed a heuristic could be introduced which shows
good results, but is not correct in every instance. The other purpose is to guide
the search such that that the ﬁrst, if any, solution found has certain character-
istics. One example would be to prioritise what is expected to the the shortest
combined path of all trains, minimising the combined travel distance. Another
example could be to de-prioritise stop and go scenarios, which in a real would
scenario would have large fuel consumption.
The two kinds of state, Controller and Conductor would implement prioritisa-
tion diﬀerently. Prioritising Conductor states is very natural as only a single
winning state is to be found. As such the siblings of a Controller state does not
need to be searched if one is deemed winning. This would not make sense for
Conductor states, because all Conductor siblings would need to be determined
winning for the predecessor to be winning. As such prioritising the Conductor
state most likely to win does not make much sense. Here the opposite could be
done, prioritising the state least likely to win. If such a state is found, then the
remaining siblings would not need to be searched.
==PAGE:58==
46
Reduction of Game Size
(a) Simulation: Best Case
(b) Simulation: Worst Case
Figure 4.1: Game Simulations
In this solution only Controller states are prioritised as described. Introducing
prioritisation does however have the downside of reducing the usefulness of lazy
evaluation. In order to sort a list, every element needs to be evaluated enough
to perform the sorting, before the ﬁrst element of the sorted list can be retrieved.
The parameters needed for prioritisation is generated slightly diﬀerently from
the sub-ﬁlter parameters. The relevant Controller successor technique described
in section 4.1, generates a Controller state by choosing a train and simulating
its movement to the next linear segment. This information is provided to the
priority, meaning Controller states are prioritises based on the expected train
movement. There is however a risk that another train will move. As such prior-
ities could have been made such that a Controller state is prioritised according
to all possible train movements, similarly to the sub-ﬁlter.
Figure 4.1 shows an example where the number of states explored depends on
the state prioritisation. Figure 4.1a shows the best case simulation, in which
case the Controller states are prioritised such that the red train can move to its
goal while blue is forced to stay in place. Afterwards the blue train is allowed
to move to its goal. In this prioritisation there are no simulated conﬂicts and as
such the smallest possible number of states is explored. Note that the dotted
lines is a shorthand for a consecutive series of states where the train in question
moves.
==PAGE:59==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
47
Figure 4.1b shows the same game, where the movement of the blue train is pri-
oritised. In this case the blue train attempts to move to goal. The red train is
however blocking this goal, the resulting simulation shows that the blue train
needs to be forced to stay in its initial position before red can move to goal.
The resulting plan is identical to the one found in ﬁgure 4.1a, the number of
states explored to ﬁnd this solution is however larger.
4.2.1
All Pairs Shortest Path
One approach which can be used to ﬁlter out redundant states is to represent
the railway network as a directed graph, and solve the All-Pairs-Shortest-Path
(APSP) problem. By solving the APSP problem for a directed graph, the min-
imal distance between each vertex will be calculated.
The purpose of solving APSP for a railway network is to identify if a train has
reached a position, from where it is impossible to reach the goal. If such a state
is identiﬁed, then further exploration becomes meaningless. Figure 4.2a shows
a simple railway network, assuming that the railway does not stop at l3, and
that there is large stretch of railway connected to it. Imagine that a state GSn
is encountered where Tpos(GSn, tblue) = l3. It can trivially be seen that a goal
state cannot be reached from here. This is because the Tdst(tblue) = l2 and there
is no possible way for the train to move from l3 to l2. As such whenever such a
state is encountered further exploration can be ceased.
In order to solve the APSP problem, the railway ﬁrst needs to be represented as
an acyclic directed graph (DAG). This is achieved by using all linear segments
as vertices. The edges between the vertices are deﬁned as either two linear seg-
ments being connected directly, or indirectly connected, separated by a junction.
It should be noted that all edges are assumed to have weight 1. Once this graph
has been constructed then APSP can be calculated for all linear segments. As
such, if dist represents a function L ∗L −→R, mapping two linear segments to
the shortest distance possible between these two. If a state GSn is encountered
where there exists a train t ∈T such that dist(Tpos(GSn, t), Tdst(t)) = ∞, where
∞means there is no path, then this state will never reach a goal.
Some care should however be taken when constructing the DAG. One solution
could be to turn the entire railway into a single graph. This would result in the
DAG shown on ﬁgure 4.2b. This is a problem as a transitive relation is created
==PAGE:60==
48
Reduction of Game Size
(a) APSP: Railway
(b) APSP: Bidirectional DAG
(c) APSP: Left Connections DAG
(d) APSP: Right Connections DAG
Figure 4.2: APSP: Transformation Demonstration
between l2 and l3, which would mean that dist(l3, l2) ̸= ∞, which results in an
inaccurate result. This result would only be a problem in-so-far that it would
not be strict enough in the end.
One way to solve this problem is to represent the railway model as two DAG’s.
One DAG is only constructed based on the connections Cleft, while the other
DAG is constructed based on Cright. This would result in the DAG’s shown
on ﬁgure 4.2c and 4.2d. Both of these, the problem on unintended transitive
relations is solved. The function dist should as such be modiﬁed such that it
also receives a direction based on the direction of the train in question.
One algorithm which can be used to solve the APSP problem is the Floyd-
Warshall algorithm [Flo62]. This algorithm makes use of dynamic programming
techniques and has a worst case time complexity of O(|Vapsp|3), and worst case
space complexity of O(|Vapsp|2), where Vapsp = L. The time complexity of trans-
forming a railway is linear in the size of the railway, as each linear segment and
junction is considered once. It should however be noted that the algorithm is run
twice, but the asymptotic complexities remain the same. The APSP informa-
tion need only be calculated once, before the start of the game exploration itself.
==PAGE:61==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
49
Another possible use of the APSP information is for path prioritisation. When-
ever a set of successor states needs to be prioritised it makes sense to prioritise
the state with the shortest possible path to goal. Although the focus of this
project is not to construct the most eﬃcient plans, it is still a nice side-eﬀect.
The more important eﬀect of this prioritisation is, that if the shortest paths
are prioritised then the expected search search space is reduced. If a state is
encountered where the shortest distance to goal is 5, then the train needs to
move at least that many positions. Given that Conductor only makes every
second move, this means that at least 10 states are needed before the train is in
goal. As such it makes sense to prioritise the shortest expected path. There may
be situations where the shortest expected path is in fact not the correct path.
Given that no other prioritisation exists at this point, choosing the correct path
ﬁrst would simply be luck based anyway.
4.2.2
Overlapping Paths
One attempt at ﬁnding a way to prioritise states is by identifying bottlenecks
in the railway network. A bottleneck can be deﬁned in diﬀerent ways, yielding
diﬀerent behaviours. Here an estimate of a bottleneck is introduced, called over-
lapping paths. An overlapping path is deﬁned as a relation between two trains
moving in opposite directions. An overlapping path is a continuous stretch of
the railway where both trains have a common potentially valid path. Here po-
tentially valid means that assuming no other trains are present, the train should
be able to reach its goal from every segment in this path.
The overlapping paths are deﬁned statically, meaning they are generated be-
fore the start of the game. In order to do this, all trains are partitioned into
two sets based on their facing direction. All valid paths are then calculated for
each train, resulting in a set of paths for each train. The overlapping paths
are then calculated by creating the cartesian product of the trains in the two
train-partitions. For each train pair, a cartesian product is calculated for each
of their valid paths, resulting in a set of path pairs. The paths are then regarded
as strings and All-Common-Substrings are then found for the path pairs. All-
Common-Substrings can be found via the dynamic programming solution to the
Longest-Common-Substring problem described in [Wik]. Rather than returning
the longest substring, the table of substrings is built. Afterwards it is possible
to iterate through the table in reverse order to collect all common substrings.
It should be noted that one of the paths in the pair is reversed before the sub-
strings are found as the trains are going in opposite directions. It should also be
noted that each character-position in a string is only present in one substring,
meaning if the common substring ’abc’ was found then the same ’a’ character
==PAGE:62==

will not be present in other common substrings. The resulting substrings are
the overlapping paths. Lastly each train is associated with all substrings/over-
lapping paths found for each pair the train was present in. The symbols used
for the deﬁnition of overlapping paths can be see in table 4.1.
Once the overlapping paths have been created, a Controller vertex can be priori-
tised based on the train expected to move and the expected destination. This
information is present due to the successor function described in section 4.1.
The overlapping paths for the moving train is found, if the expected destination
is inside an overlapping path, and one of the opposite moving trains sharing
that overlapping path is already inside that same path, then the state is de-
prioritized. This is because there is an expected higher probability of collision.
This cannot be used as a ﬁlter, because there exists a possibility of the opposite
moving train leaving the overlapping path via a junction.
Figure 4.3 shows two very similar examples, with the same overlapping path,
which is marked on the ﬁgures. Figure 4.3a shows an example where initially
the movement of the red and blue train is prioritised equally. Assuming the blue
train moves ﬁrst, then next the blue train is about to move into an overlapping
==PAGE:63==

51
(a) Overlapping Path Ideal Scenario
(b) Overlapping Path Problem Scenario
Figure 4.3: Overlapping Path Scenarios
path which is already occupied by the red train. It can trivially be seen that
the blue train cannot reach its goal before the red train has left the path. The
heuristic will in this case prioritise the movement of the red train until it has
left the path with results in the smallest possible fraction of the search space
being explored.
Figure 4.3b shows the same example. In this situation however the signal stop-
ping the blue train from entering the overlapping path has been removed. The
result is that the movement of the blue train is ﬁrst de-prioritised at a point
where it cannot be stopped. The result is that the heuristic has become useless
as it does not take into account whether the train in question can be controlled.
4.2.3
True Bottlenecks
The overlapping path heuristic served as an estimation for identifying bottle-
necks in the railway.
The concept has its ﬂaws, given that an overlapping
path may not be a true bottleneck, meaning two trains can enter simultane-
ously and still ﬁnd a solution. As such there is a risk the the prioritisation is
counter-productive in some scenarios. The other aspect is that it is only used
for prioritisation combined with the fact it is not taken into account if a train
can be controlled, results in situations where prioritisation does not aﬀect the
game exploration in any way.
==PAGE:64==
52
Reduction of Game Size
Figure 4.4: Bottleneck Game Example
Given these problems it would be desirable to reﬁne the overlapping paths such
that only true bottlenecks are left. In this case a bottleneck would still be an
overlapping path between two trains. The diﬀerence is that it should only be
used if there exists no solution to the game once both trains has entered the
path. If it is possible to identify these true bottlenecks then counter-productive
eﬀects on the search space disappear. Another important aspect is, that rather
than prioritising states it becomes possible to ﬁlter states. If two trains have
entered a true bottleneck then no solution exists. As such there is no need to
ever explore the possible movements inside the bottleneck. This also negates
the fact that the overlapping paths does not take into account if a train is con-
trollable as the state is ﬁltered.
The simplest way of reﬁning the overlapping paths, is to solve a modiﬁed ver-
sion of the game. If an overlapping path has been found, then the endpoints
of this path can be extracted. A new game is created based on the two trains,
and endpoints. The railway network remains unchanged, but the new game
only has the two trains in question present. The two trains will have the same
destination and direction as in the original game, but their initial positions will
be that of the endpoints. The example shown on ﬁgure 4.3b will yield the game
shown on ﬁgure 4.4. This new small game is now solved using the standard
game solver. The value of the initial vertex is then found in the resulting ﬁxed-
point assignment. If the value is 0 then no solution exists and the overlapping
path has been proven to be a true bottleneck, assuming the correctness of the
game solver. If the value was 1 then a solution exists and the overlapping path
is discarded.
==PAGE:65==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
53
The game shown on ﬁgure 4.4 is not solvable as such the overlapping path is a
true bottleneck. Knowing this, if the example shown on ﬁgure 4.3b is revisited.
Once the train is about to enter the bottleneck, the train cannot be stopped.
This does however not matter, because it is now certain that no solutions exist.
As such the state can be ﬁltered out before exploring it.
The downside of this approach is that the cost of ﬁnding these true bottle-
necks is potentially very high. It has already been discussed how the game size
is exponential relative to the size of the railway and number of trains. The
new small games will as such worst case have an exponential time complexity.
These smaller games will however only ever have two trains making the prob-
lem easier. Another aspect is that solving the sub-games can make use of all
optimisations described in this chapter, except for the bottlenecks themselves,
potentially making the games trivial to solve.
The main concern is the cost of calculating the overlapping paths in the ﬁrst
place. Calculating the valid paths for a train can be done by simply tracing the
possible train routes through the railway. This means that the time complexity
of calculating one path for the train has a time complexity of O(|R|). Each
train could however have multiple paths, meaning the cost is linear for each
path. Given that each train has to reach a single goal, the only way for a train
to have multiple possible paths is via junctions. Each junction potentially splits
a single path into two paths, meaning the worst case number of paths per train
is |J| + 1. As such the time complexity for calculating all valid paths becomes
O(|R| · |J| · |T|).
Next is to ﬁnd the overlapping paths. Given that the overlapping paths are
calculated for opposite moving trains, the worst case number of train matches
is if half of the trains face one way, and half the other, resulting in (|T|
2 )2 po-
tential train pairs. Each train worst case has O(|J|) paths meaning there are
worst case O(|J|2) path pairs for each train pair. The ﬁnal result is worst case
O(|T|2 · |J|2) path pairs. All-Common-Substrings are calculated for each path
pair. Assuming that the algorithm described in [Wik] is used, The calculation
of All-Common-Substrings has a time complexity of O(|N|·|R|) where N and R
is the two strings. As Each path will worst case contain all linear segments. The
complexity of calculating these common substrings becomes O(|T|2 · |J|2 · |L|2).
The worst case number of common substrings produced is O(|L|2) for each train,
because each pair of linear segments could represent the endpoints for an over-
lapping path, but overlapping paths cannot be nested inside each other. As
such there will worst case be O(|T|2 · |L|2) overlapping paths. Each of these
==PAGE:66==
54
Reduction of Game Size
overlapping paths are then turned into a game and solved.
Assuming quasi-polynomial time complexity when solving the sub-games with
optimisations applied, the complexity of calculating the true bottlenecks re-
mains polynomial. It is however worst case still a very expensive computation,
especially if the games are not easily solved.
Theorem 4.1 A bottleneck is deﬁned as a totally ordered set over a con-
tinuous stretch of linear segments LOP , relative to two opposite facing trains

The ends of the stretch are deﬁned as lleft and lright.
A
game can be constructed where the two trains are facing the same direction

Tsrc(tright) = lright. If the LiuSmolka game solver is run on this game and the
result of F G
min(v0) = 0, where v0 is the initial state, then the bottleneck has been
determined to be a true bottleneck.
If a state GSbottle0 is encountered in the full game, where Tpos(GSbottle0, tleft) =
lleft and Tpos(GSbottle0, tright) = lright, then it can be determined that F G

2. Tpos(GSbottle1, tright) = lright and Tpos(GSbottle1, tleft) ∈LOP .

other train is somewhere inside the stretch. Then it can also be determined that

Proof. Assuming the correctness of the LiuSmolka game solver, then it trivially
holds that F G

solutions for this conﬁguration of the two trains. If this situation is encountered
in the full game, then this remains true, as adding more trains cannot solve the
local problem for tleft and tright.
The second statement F G
min(GSbottle1) = 0 also holds. This is because the a state
GSbottle1 will always be reachable from a state GSbottle0. As such if there could

4.2.4
Uncontrollable Stretches
Another heuristic is the ’uncontrollable stretches’ heuristic. Initially all possi-
ble paths are calculated for each train. Each of these paths are then broken
into stretches where Controller cannot aﬀect the movement of a train. In other
words once a train has entered one of these stretches, there are no meaningful
choices Controller can make, aﬀecting the train in question.
Calculating the uncontrollable stretches is performed before the exploration of
the game graph is started, meaning it operates purely on the railway deﬁnition.
An uncontrollable stretch is a set of continuous linear segments, and applies to
a single train.
==PAGE:68==
56
Reduction of Game Size
Figure 4.5 shows examples of the 5 basic rules for constructing the uncontrol-
lable stretches. It should be noted that the brackets indicate the stretches and
only linear segments are labelled. The calculation of an uncontrollable stretch
starts on the train start position and follows the railway in the train direction.
Rule 1: If the next track segment is a linear segment, and they are not separated
by a signal facing in the direction of the train, the next segment is added to
the current stretch. This is because Controller cannot take an action which
aﬀects the movement of the train. Figure 4.5a shows an example where
the calculation starts at l11. The next element l12 is a linear segment and
there are no signal between l11 and l12 as such l12 is added to the stretch.
Similarly there is no signal between l12 and l13 as such this segment is also
added to the stretch. Final result is the stretch [l11, l12, l13].
Rule 2: If a signal is encountered in the direction of the train the current stretch
is closed. A new stretch is then started from the next liner segment. This
is because there is now a possibility of stopping the train representing a
meaningful choice. Figure 4.5b shows an example where a stretch [l11, l12]
is created according to rule 1. A signal is encountered when leaving l12,
meaning the next linear segment l13 forms the start of a new stretch. The
ﬁnal result is the stretches [l11, l12] and [l13, l14].
Rule 3: If a junction is encountered and the entrance point is either pup or pdown
then the stretch construction moves to the next segment without breaking
the current stretch. This is because; although Controller can control the
junction, if the junction is not connected to the point from which the train
enters, then the train will derail if moved. As such choosing to connect
the junction to the opposite point does not constitute a meaningful choice.
Figure 4.5c shows an example where the train starts at l21. A junction is
then entered from the up point meaning the stretch is not broken. The
segments l12 and l13 are then added to the stretch because of rule 1. The
result is the stretch [l21, l12, l13].
Rule 4: If a junction is encountered where the train enters from pstem then there
is the possibility of a meaningful choice. If it is known beforehand that
one of the paths will never result in a solution then the stretch remains
unbroken.
This is because choosing a path which is already known to
fail does not constitute a meaningful choice. The canonical way to detect
a failing path is via the All-Pairs-Shortest-Path problem as described in
4.2.1. Figure 4.5d shows and example where the train starts at l11. The
linear segment l12 is added due to rule 1. A junction is entered from the
stem direction. It can be detected through the use of All-Pairs-Shortest-
Path that there is no path to l21 from l13. As such the other path l21 is
added to the stretch. The result is the stretch [l11, l12, l21].
==PAGE:69==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States

Figure 4.6 shows a larger example where all rules are used, note that the junc-
tions are now labelled. Initially the stretch [l11, l12] is created according to rule
1.
The junction j1 is encountered which corresponds to rule 5 and as such
breaks the stretch. If the up exits is followed then the stretch [l21, l22, l16] is
created.
Junction j2 does not break the stretch according to rule 4, and j3
does not break the stretch according to rule 3. The stretch is stopped at l16
as there is a signal meaning rule 2 is invoked. The down exits of j1 results in
the stretch [l13, l14, l15, l16] due to rule 1, 2 and 3. Finally the stretch [l17, l18]
is created. The ﬁnal result is 4 uncontrollable stretches: [l11, l12], [l21, l22, l16],
[l13, l14, l15, l16] and [l17, l18].
==PAGE:70==
58
Reduction of Game Size
Figure 4.6: Combination of Rules
The time complexity of calculating these stretches for each train, is equal to all
linear segments which can occur in a valid path for that train, plus the point,
junctions and signals with is encountered in such a path. Given that each linear
segment could potentially be part of a valid path of a train the worst case time
complexity is O(|R|). This is done for each train meaning the ﬁnal time com-
plexity for the initial calculation is O(|T|·|R|). The worst case space complexity
of storing the stretches is O(|T|·|L|). When looking at a single train, each linear
segment will at most be part of a single uncontrollable stretch. As such each
stretch could worst case be part of an uncontrollable stretch for each train.
Prioritisation of Controller states are performed as follows: If the train in ques-
tion is about to enter into an uncontrollable stretch where there is already a train
moving in the opposite direction, then the Controller state is de-prioritised. This
de-prioritisation is made because; if the opposite moving train is not moved out
of the stretch and the train in question enters the stretch, then Controller can-
not prevent the train from colliding with the opposite moving train.
This version has two advantages over the bottleneck heuristic. The ﬁrst is that
the heuristic is based on the placement of signals and junctions, meaning if
a Controller state is prioritised then there is always the possibility of making
a meaningful decision.
This can be done either by stopping the train via a
signal, or by choosing the other junction path. Another improvement is that
two stretches, for a single train, cannot overlap.
One possible downside relative to the bottleneck heuristic, is that the uncon-
trollable stretches are deﬁned on a per train basis, where the bottlenecks were
deﬁned relative to two trains. This means that one train may be allowed to move
into a stretch because there are no trains in the opposite direction in the stretch.
But looking further ahead it can be seen that a conﬂict will arise, an example
of this can be seen on ﬁgure 4.7. This ﬁgure shows the same game as ﬁgure 4.1
with uncontrollable stretch information. It can be seen that both trains start in
a stretch. Both trains will initially move into a free stretch, as such the heuristic
cannot prioritise the trains. The ﬁgure shows a worst case situation where the
==PAGE:71==
4.2 Filtering & Prioritising States
59
Figure 4.7: Uncontrollable Stretches: Problem
blue train is prioritised and only once it is about to enter the third stretch does
the heuristic see a problem, but at this point it is too late as the blue train is
already blocking the red train. The ﬁgure also demonstrates that adding more
signals to a railway reduces the performance of the heuristic. The if signal sepa-
rating the second and third blue stretches was not present, these two would form
one large stretch and the heuristic would know to prioritise the red train initially.
4.2.5
Uncontrollable Stretches as Filters
The uncontrollable stretch heuristic described in 4.2.4 was based on the concept
of constructing stretches of railway track where a train could be meaningfully
aﬀected by Controller. These stretches are used as a heuristic; de-prioritising
Controller states which allow a train to move into an uncontrollable stretch
where an opposite moving train is already present. In general this heuristic has
yielded promising results sparking interest in further exploration of this concept.
The original idea of the heuristic was that if a train tUS entered an occupied
uncontrollable stretch LUS, then the likelihood of a train collision is large, as
tUS cannot be stopped. The thought however was that the opposite train top
may move out of the way because of a junction, meaning a collision is not
guaranteed.
This statement is true but forgets one of the basic premises of
the problem; the construction of a correct-by-construction plan, said in other
words the movement of the trains are random, and as such the plan needs to
be correct no matter how the trains move as long as the basic rules of the game
are followed. If this premise is combined with the idea of the heuristic, then it
becomes apparent that the concept can actually be used to remove states rather
==PAGE:72==
60
Reduction of Game Size
than de-prioritising them.
The idea is; if a Controller state GSn allows a train tUS to enter an occupied
uncontrollable stretch LUS then there exists a path through the game graph
where the collision occurs no matter what moves Controller make from GSn.
Based on this it can be inferred that GSn will never be part of the ﬁnal plan as
it needs to guarantee that no collision occurs. Given that GSn will not be part
of the plan, then expanding and exploring GSn is superﬂuous. Another small
point of interest is that in the heuristic version it was assumed that for a train
top to block an uncontrollable stretch, it needed to move in the opposite direc-
tion of tUS. Due to the same argument this is not the case as there exists a path
where top stands still and tUS moves, eventually resulting in tUS moving into top.
Theorem 4.2 Assuming an uncontrollable stretch US as deﬁned in table 4.2.
For all states GSn where Tpos(GSn, tUS) ∈LUS and there exists a train top ̸=
tUS where Tpos(GSn, top) ∈LUS. Then it will always hold that F G
min(GSn) = 0
as Controller cannot prevent a collision from this state. As such there is no
need to further explore this state.
Proof. The elements in tUS forms the total order [US0
i , US1
i ...USn
i ]. Assume a
state GSConductor(0) where Tpos(GSConductor(0), tUS) = US0
i and Tpos(GSConductor(0), top) =
USm
i
where 0 ≤m ≤n.
Based on the game rules deﬁned in section 3.2 and the knowledge that Controller
cannot aﬀect the movement of tUS while Tpos(GSConductor(0), tUS) ∈LUS: No
matter what move Controller makes from GSConductor(0) there will exist a Con-
ductor successor GSConductor(1) where Tpos(GSConductor(1), tUS) = US1
i and
Tpos(GSConductor(1), top) = USm
i , meaning tUS moves while top does not. This
holds true until GSConductor(n+1). This results in an unavoidable sequence of
states where tUS moves through all segments of LUS, while top remains station-
ary. In this sequence of states there must exist a state GSConductor(m) where
Tpos(GSConductor(m), tUS) = Tpos(GSConductor(m), top) = USm
i
which forms a
collision. As such all states in this sequence will eventually result in a collision,
be a collision or be a successor of a collision.
As such F G
min(GSConductor(k)) = 0 for all k ∈{0..n}.

Chapter 5
Solution Architecture
This chapter will provide a high level view of the implementation. What are
the important types and how are they deﬁned. Additionally the code domains
are shown and their interaction is described. The names of some functions are
changed from the actual code, to be more clarifying.
5.1
Core Types
In the core of the solution there exists 4 type domains. The ﬁrst type domain
shown on ﬁgure 5.1 represents the types used in the deﬁnition of the railway
model. All types shown are parameterised over label and point. This means that
these types could be instantiated as anything. There are however the constraint
that the types need to form total orders and be hashable. This parametisation
exists for two reasons. There exists multiple types which could be used, such as
Strings and Integers, but as shown later sometimes tuples are used, representing
values in namespaces. The simplest solution would be to deﬁne both as String,
this however runs the risk of mixing their usage in the implementation. By
writing the parameterised code, diﬀerent usage is enforced.
==PAGE:74==
62
Solution Architecture
Figure 5.1: Railway Types
The type Linear represents a linear segment. The deﬁnition is almost identical
to that of the model.
The type itself is deﬁned as a record, containing two
points and a label. The type Switch represents a junction from the model. This
type is also deﬁned as a record, containing a label and three points. The type
Segment is a synthetic type which is deﬁned as a union over Linear and Switch,
this is used when deﬁning connections. The ﬁnal simple type is Signal, which
represents a signal. It is deﬁned as a tuple of a Linear and a point. In the ﬁgure
it is given record notation for the sake of clarity.
The type RailwayDeﬁnition represents the entire railway model. The deﬁni-
tion is similar to the model. The components of the railway if deﬁned in the
4 sets: points, linearSegments, switchSegments and signals. The connections
leftToRightConnections and rightToLeftConnections are deﬁned as maps from
point’s to Segment’s, which is roughly analogous to a set of tuples, used in the
model.
The second type domain shown on ﬁgure 5.2 shows the types used in the deﬁni-
tion of the railway game. It should be noted that this domain has knowledge of
the types used in the railway model and is also parameterised over train. The
ﬁrst type is Direction which represents the two possible directions LeftToRight
and RightToLeft.
The second type TrainAttributes is a tuple containing the
initial conditions of a train: What direction it is facing, what its initial position
is and what its destination is. TrainAttributes is given record notation in the
ﬁgure for clarity. Finally GameDeﬁnition represents the entire game, and con-
==PAGE:75==
5.1 Core Types
63
Figure 5.2: Game Types
Figure 5.3: Game State Types
sists of a single map, mapping trains to their initial conditions. The keys of the
map as such implicitly deﬁnes what trains are present.
The third type domain shown on ﬁgure 5.3 shows the types used for the game
graph. The type Player represents the two possible players Controller and Con-
ductor. The type GamePosition represents the position of a train. This type is
equal to Linear except an additional value Derail is added. The value Derial is
a meta-position and represents all positions which is not within the railway, and
as such is a derailment. The type GState represents a vertex in the game graph
and as such encodes the state of the entire railway.
latestMoveBy indicates
which player made the move, and as such indicates if the state is a member of
VConductor or VController. greenSignals is a set of all signals which are currently
green, upSwitches is a set of all junctions which are set to up. A GState is im-
plicitly deﬁned based on the RailwayDeﬁnition and RailwayGame, as such the
red signals and down junctions are implicitly deﬁned as the remaining elements
of signals and switchSegments. The last member is trainPositions which maps
all trains to its current GamePosition.
==PAGE:76==
64
Solution Architecture
Figure 5.4: LiuSmolka Types
The ﬁnal type domain shown on ﬁgure 5.4 represents the types used in the LiuS-
molka computation. These types are parameterised over v which represents the
type of the vertices. The type HyperEdge is a synonym for a tuple, consisting
of a vertex and a list of vertices. The lone vertex represents the source vertex
of a hyper-edge and the list of vertices represent the destination set. The set
is represented as a list here because duplicates are assumed to not be present,
and even if they were it would not be a problem. The type LiuSmolkaState
represents what would be the global LiuSmolka state. Here it is represented as
a value, where each iteration of the LiuSmolka algorithm creates a new instance,
rather than modifying a global state. The LiuSmolka state contains w which is a
list of pending hyper-edges. The member a is a map from a vertex to a Boolean,
representing the current ﬁxed-point estimate assignment. The value d is a map
from a vertex to a list of HyperEdge’s, representing the vertex dependencies as
deﬁned by the LiuSmolka algorithm.
5.2
Game Domains
The general code domains used by the game and their interaction can be seen
on ﬁgure 5.5 and consists of 3 main logical modules. It should be noted that the
type parameters are not shown here for the sake of conciseness. First the en-
tity labelled StaticDeﬁnitions consists of the various static information globally
available during the game exploration. At the moment this contains Railway-
Deﬁnition and GameDeﬁnition.
Before the modules and their constituent functions are described it is important
to note that most of the functions returns values of the form m a. The preﬁx
==PAGE:77==
5.2 Game Domains
65
Figure 5.5: Simple Program Architecture
m represents a monad and for the time it can be seen as a way to introduce
semi-global information via the reader monad. In other words m a is currently
synonymous with StaticDefinitions −→a. The results as such represents func-
tions which calculate a value once provided the StaticDeﬁnitions meaning they
have access to the constituent parts of this structure. Another thing to note
about the type signature is that lower case types represent type parameters,
while the upper case types represent concrete types.
The ﬁrst module is the Railway Game this module logically contains two impor-
tant components. The ﬁrst is initialState which calculates the ﬁrst game state
based on the RailwayDeﬁnition and GameDeﬁnition. The second is allSucces-
sors which represents a function calculating all successor states of a given state.
The notation [a] means a list of type a. In other words Railway Game contains
the logic for creating the game graph as described in section 3.2.
The second module LiuSmolka contains the implementation of the version of
the LiuSmolka algorithm described in section 2.2.5. This module is deﬁned in-
dependently meaning it could be used calculate the ﬁxed-point assignment of
arbitrary dependency graphs. The module has one primary function liuSmolka-
Local. It should be noted that n represents a monad similarly to m but here is
agnostic as to what the monad represents. It is only present to allow an arbi-
trary monad to be injection into the function. The function works on vertices
of type v. The ﬁrst function parameter is another function which is used to ex-
pand the graph. The input function receives a vertex which is to be expanded,
and results in a list of lists of vertices. The reason for nesting lists is that each
inner list forms the destination vertices for a HyperEdge. As such the function
==PAGE:78==
66
Solution Architecture
returns a list of destination sets (here modelled as a list). The input parameter
for the function implicitly represents the source vertex of all resulting Hyper-
Edge’s. The second parameter for liusSmolkaLocal represents v0 in the local

the ﬁxed-point assignment. In the resulting assignment A the following holds:
A(v) = False
=⇒
F G

=⇒

Said
in other words liuSmolkaLocal represents a function G −→A but rather than
representing a dependency graph as a set of vertices and a set of hyper-edges
(V, E) as described in 2.2, it is represented as an initial vertex and a function
which represents a hyper-edge adjacency list.
The ﬁnal module Combined Game, as the name implies, represents the combi-
nation of the railway game and the LiuSmolka algorithm and has access to all
public information from these two. The module has two main functions. The
ﬁrst function allSuccessorsDep is a modiﬁed version of allSucessors from Rail-
way Game. This function simply transforms the output of allSuccessors, which
does not have any concept of what a dependency graph is, into a form which
liuSmolkaLocal understands as described in section 3.4. The second function
signalPlan receives a ﬁxed-point assignment from LiuSmolka and transforms it
into a signalling plan.
The normal ﬂow for solving a game would be to initially call the function
liuSmolkaLocal with allSuccessorsDep and initialState, followed by calling sig-
nalPlan with the result of liuSmolkaLocal. As such when calling liuSmolkaLocal
the following type substitutions occur: { v −→GState, n −→m }. This is all
performed inside the m monad, allowing the composition of monadic values.
The structure described in relation to ﬁgure 5.5 represents a simpliﬁed version
which does not take any of the optimisations discussed in chapter 4 into account.
Figure 5.6 shows and updated version which does take the optimisations into
account. The main diﬀerence is the addition of allSuccessorsAdv in Railway
Game and the modiﬁed signature of allSuccessorsDep in Combined Game. all-
SuccessorsAdv represents the modiﬁed successor function described in section
4.1 where only relevant Controller states are generated. This version of the
function also receives a Filter and a Priority as described in section 4.2.
This version of the function ﬁlters and prioritises Controller states as described
is section 4.2. The advantage of this approach is that if a Filter and Priority
is partially applied to allSuccessorsAdv then the signature becomes identical to
allSuccessors.
==PAGE:79==
5.2 Game Domains
67
Figure 5.6: Program Architecture
The signature of allSuccessorsDep is modiﬁed such that it receives an arbitrary
successor function and transforms it into a hyper-edge function.
The result
is further decomposition of the abstractions. allSuccessorsDep does not have
any understanding of how successors are transformed, it simply knows how to
transform a normal graph into a dependency graph. There now exists several
options for generating the successors. The naive allSuccessors can be used, or
allSuccessorsAdv can be used with an arbitrary Filter and Priority.
The ﬂow for solving a game has been slightly changed.
Rather than invok-
ing allSuccessorsDep directly, a successor function is created and injected into
allSuccessorsDep, which can then be injected into LiuSmolkaLocal as previously.
Finally StaticDeﬁnitions are also expanded to contain the various properties
which are calculated based on the railway model and/or railway game. These
being the All-Pairs-Shortest-Path information described in section 4.2.1, the
bottleneck information described in section 4.2.2 and the uncontrollable stretch
information described in section 4.2.4.
==PAGE:80==
