Solution Architecture
Figure 5.7: LiuSmolka Monad Stack
5.3
Monad Stack
Many of the interfaces in the project has a type parameter representing a monad.
This serves several purposes in the architecture. In the functional world there
are diﬀerent ways to introduce abstractions into the code. One way is via higher
order functions, or by partially applying values to functions in order to create
more specialised versions.
Some abstractions can be captured by a monad.
There are several advantages associated with monads. It is in general possible
to combine two monads, resulting in a new monad. By stacking several monads,
a stack of abstractions can be created, usually called monad transformers.
Throughout the project diﬀerent monad stacks are used. The interfaces used
when solving the game tends to expect input values to be present inside a monad,
without specifying which monad. This allows each module to capture their own
abstractions in a monad, and allows the generation of modules which accepts
a monad, then proceeds to apply its own stack of monads on top of the input
monad. This module speciﬁc layer of monads can then be removed before the
ﬁnal value is created, resulting in an output captured inside the input monad.
Figure 5.7 shows the monad stack used for the calculation of the LiuSmolka
algorithm. The algorithm is separated into three sections: LiuSmolka, Rail-
wayGame and Base. These sections describe were in the program the monads
are applied.
==PAGE:81==
5.3 Monad Stack
69
The base of the monad stack is either Identity or IO. These are the two common
base monads for any monad stack. In general none of the modules makes use of
IO actions, meaning Identity is in general ﬁne. In order to measure time spent,
for experimental purposes, the base monad can be exchanged with IO such that
the test has access to to system clock and similar. This injection of IO can as
such be achieved without any of the other modules knowing.
The second layer is the RailwayGame. This layer is added once the game be-
gins, meaning it is added as soon as the calculation of the StaticDeﬁnitions are
complete. The monad used is a reader monad in which the static deﬁnitions are
stored. This means that all calculations performed inside this monad can treat
the StaticDeﬁnitions as globally available information. This monad is generally
referred to as the railway monad.
The ﬁnal layer shown is the LiuSmolka layer. This layer is added inside of the
function liuSmolkaLocal. This allows the LiuSmoka algorithm to create its own
monadic abstractions on top of the ones receives from the outside. The liuS-
molkaLocal algorithm accepts any monad, meaning it does not make explicit
use of the global information in StaticData. The hyper-edge function it receives
however creates its results inside this monad, meaning the hyper-edge function
can fully rely on StaticData without making liuSmolkaLocal aware. The LiuS-
molka layer uses two monads internally. The ﬁrst monad is a reader, containing
the initial vertex, and the hyper-edge function, making these globally available
inside LiuSmolka. The second monad is a state monad, containing the LiuS-
molkaState. This allows the LiuSmolka algorithm to be deﬁned in a way which
emulates the usage of mutable imperative state, similar to how the algorithm is
deﬁned, in a pure functional manner. The LiuSmolka layer is removed once the
execution of liuSmolkaLocal local is ﬁnished.
These are not the only monads used in the implementation. But the general
pattern is that the Base layer is used globally. The RailwayGame layer is added
after the calculation of StaticData. LiuSmolka uses it own layer internally, and
some modules uses their own layer on top of RailwayGame. Another example
is the calculation of allSuccessorsAdv which uses its own monads internally.
==PAGE:82==
70
Solution Architecture
Figure 5.8: Filtering & Prioritisation
5.4
Railway Pre-Processing
This section describes the domains associated with the optimisations introduced
in section 4.2. How are they constructed, and how are they used in the game.
Figure 5.8 shows the the general types and functions used for ﬁltering and pri-
oritisation. The domain Filtering & Prioritisation deﬁnes the types and basic
operations which can be performed on these.
The type Filter represents a full ﬁlter, receiving a GState and producing a
Boolean, stating if the state should be ﬁltered or not. If the Filter returns True
then the state is retained. If the Filter returns False then the state can be ﬁl-
tered out. The type SubFilter represents a ﬁlter applied to a GState, a train and
a Linear representing a possible Conductor successor state. A SubFilter as such
states if a successor state can be ﬁltered. The type Priortiy represents a priority
and receives the same input as SubFilter. The result is a value which should
be a member of a totally ordered type. All three types returns their value in-
side the railway monad. This means that the results can be based on StaticData.
The function createFilterM is used to compose two Filter’s, resulting in a ﬁlter
which forms the conjunction over the results of the constituent Filter’s. the
function createSubFilterM is similar except it composes SubFilters. The func-
tion createPriorityM is used to compose Priority’s. The resulting Priority will
==PAGE:83==
5.4 Railway Pre-Processing
71
Figure 5.9: All-Pairs-Shortest-Path
ﬁrst sort according to the ﬁrst value, followed by the second.
The function allSuccessorsFilter is used to transform a SubFilter into a Filter.
The resulting Filter receives a Controller state, and calculates all possible train
movements. All movements are mapped through the SubFilter. The ﬁnal result
is the conjunction of all train movements mapped through the SubFilter.
The type OccupationState is a value used by some priorities.
The type has
two values Free and Occupied. The type forms a total order such that Free <
Occupied. This means that states which produces Free is prioritised higher than
those producing Occupied.
Figure 5.9 shows the functions and types associated with the calculation of
All-Pairs-Shortest-Path as described in section 4.2.1. The domain All-Pairs-
Shortest-Path contains the generic implementation of the Floyd-Warshall algo-
rithm named ﬂoydWarshall. This function receives an adjacency function. The
adjacency function receives a vertex v and produces a list of destination ver-
tices, and a weight i associated with each edge. ﬂoydWarshall also receives a
list, containing all vertices. The result is a map Map (v,v) i, mapping pairs of
vertices to a weight.
The domain Railway All-Pairs-Shortest-Path contains the functions which is
able to transform a RailwayDeﬁnition into two DAG’s and invoke ﬂoydWar-
shall on them.
The type DirAPSP represents the APSP information for a
single DAG, and is identical to the output of ﬂoydWarhsall applied with the
==PAGE:84==
72
Solution Architecture
substitution rules {v −→Linear, i −→Int}. This means that the vertices in the
emerging DAG’s is the Linear’s from the RailwayDeﬁnition. The type APSP
is a collection of APSP information for both DAG’s which emerges from the
RailwayDeﬁnition and is a mapping between a Direction and a DirAPSP.
The function expandRailwayEdges is used to ﬁnd the adjacent elements of a Lin-
ear. The function receives the RailwayDeﬁnition and a Direction, and the result
is a adjacency function which is compatible with ﬂoydWarshall under the sub-
stitution rules described earlier.
The function railwayFloydWarshall receives
the RailwayDeﬁnition and produces an APSP representing the entire Floyd-
Warshall calculation on the RailwayDeﬁnition. Internally the function invokes
ﬂoydWarshall twice, once for each Direction, and collects the results. The result
of railwayFloydWarshall is stored in StaticData making it globally available in
the railway monad.
The function lookupAPSP receives a GameDeﬁnition and an APSP, a GState,
a train and a Linear. The results in a Maybe Int representing the distance from
the input Linear to the destination of the train, the value Nothing means that
the destination is unreachable. As such the function ﬁnds the DirAPSP associ-
ated with the trains Direction, and performs a lookup with the input Linear as
the ﬁrst tuple element, and the train destination as the second tuple element.
The functions prioritizeShortestPath and ﬁlterThroughFloydWarshall are slightly
modiﬁed version of lookupAPSP. Given that ﬁlters and priorities exists inside
the railway monad, the GameDeﬁnition and APSP can be extracted from Stat-
icData. prioritizeShortest path as such is a Priority where the result is equal
to the output of lookupAPSP, if the value is Nothing the largest possible Int is
produced instead. This function as such is used to prioritise the shortest path.
The function ﬁlterThroughFloydWarshall is a SubFilter used to ﬁlter out states
which has a successor state where a train cannot reach its destination. A such
the result is False if lookupAPSP produces Nothing, otherwise the result is True.
Figure 5.10 shows the domains associated with section 4.2.2 and 4.2.3. The do-
main All-Common-Substrings contains a function named allCommonSubstrings.
The function is deﬁned generically, and receives to sequences of type a. A se-
quence is a list-like functional structure with eﬃcient lookup. As such a sequence
of a represents a string. The result is a list of lists of a. Where a list of a also
represents a string, but in a non-optimised structure. As such the output is a
list of strings, representing the common substrings.
==PAGE:85==
5.4 Railway Pre-Processing
73
Figure 5.10: Overlapping Paths & Bottlenecks
The domain Bottleneck contains the functions used to calculate overlapping
paths and bottlenecks. The type Choke represents an overlapping path in a
read optimised form. ChockePoints is a map from a tuple of a train and a Lin-
ear, to a map from another train to a Chocke. This structure maps a train, and
its entrance into an overlapping path, to a mapping of opposite moving trains,
sharing the overlapping path, and the path itself. An entry (t1, l1) −→t2 −→c as
such means that the trains t1 and t2 share the overlapping path c. The train
t1 enters the overlapping path through the segment l1
The function constructOverlappingPaths calculates all overlapping paths for all
trains and collects the result into a ChockePoints, based on the RailwayDef-
inition, GameDeﬁnition and APSP. The function contructTrueBottlenecks is
similar except it only retains the true bottlenecks by constructing and solving
small games for the overlapping paths. Both the naive overlapping paths and
true bottlenecks are stored in StaticData making them available in the railway
monad.
The function destinationInOccupiedPath receives ChockePoints, a GState, a
train and a Linear. It produces a Boolean value telling if the input train and
Linear is inside an occupied overlapping path. This is done by using this pair as
as lookup key in the ChockePoints. If this mapping exists, then all trains except
the current one is looked up in the inner map. Finally the positions of all the
==PAGE:86==
74
Solution Architecture
Figure 5.11: Uncontrollable Stretchs
trains which represents a mapping in the inner map is checked for membership
in the corresponding Chocke. If any train position is a member of a Chocke, the
the result of destinationInOccupiedPath is True otherwise the result is False.
overlappingPathPrority is the Priority version of destinationInOccupiedPath,
resulting in a Occupied if the result was True. bottleneckFilter is the SubFil-
ter version of destinationInOccupiedPath and inverts the result. Meaning if the
destination is occupied, then the state is ﬁltered out.
Figure 5.11 shows the domain associated with the technique described in section
4.2.4 and 4.2.5. The type TrainStretches represents the uncontrollable stretches
found in a railway game. The type is deﬁned as a mapping from a train, to a
mapping from Linear’s to a set of Linear’s. This represents a mapping from a
train, to the entrance of an uncontrollable stretch. The stretch itself is stored
as a set for eﬃcient lookup. As such an entry t −→l −→uc means that the train
t has the uncontrollable stretch uc and enters this stretch through the segment l.
The function allUncontrolledStretches calculates TrainStretches based on Rail-
wayDeﬁnition, GameDeﬁnition and APSP. The result of allUncontrolledStretches
is stored in StaticData making it globally available in the railway monad. The
function destInOccupiedStretch is used to check if a train and Linear is inside
an occupied uncontrollable stretch. This is done by ﬁrst looking up the train,
followed by the Linear. Lastly the positions of all other trains is found. If any
of these positions is a member of the inner set, then it is occupied and the result
is True.
prioritizeUncontrollableStrech and ﬁlterThroughUncontrollableStrech is the Pri-
ority and SubFilter versions of allUncontrolledStretches. The transformations
is identical to the one described for overlapping paths/bottlenecks.
==PAGE:87==
5.5 Railway Game Construction
75
5.5
Railway Game Construction
The structures used to represent the railway model and game are primarily
records and associative arrays, both which can be constructed by hand. This is
however time consuming and prone to typing errors. As such a domain speciﬁc
language is built inside Haskell for eﬃciently deﬁning railway networks and
games.
5.5.1
Integrated Domain-Speciﬁc Language
The there are several ways in which a domain speciﬁc language (DSL) can be
deﬁned. It could be deﬁned as an entirely new language, or as in this case as
library of functions built on top of a pre-existing language. The language can
vary in how declarative the semantics are and what, if any, source language
concept it is build around.
In this case the DSL is deﬁned on top of the Haskell language. The DSL is
deﬁned as a monad, more speciﬁcally the state monad. The simple deﬁnition
of a state monad is state −→(a, state), i.e. a function which receives a state
and returns a tuple containing some value and the next state. And ﬁnally the
language is not very declarative as constructs need to be deﬁned before they are
used in the construction of other constructs.
Each of the language elements is deﬁned as a state monad where the state con-
tains the currently deﬁned railway elements. The purpose of this deﬁnition is
that each language element receives the currently deﬁned railway and returns
an updated version as well as some value used for further computation. Said
in other words each element represents a state delta. As such these elements
can naturally be composed via the monadic bind to create a full railway game
deﬁnition.
The stretch primitive indicates that a new stretch of linear segments is being
deﬁned. Here a stretch of linear segments is 1 or more continuous linear seg-
ments.
The language uses the primitive inﬁx operators (< .), (. >) (< ∗.) and (.∗>) to
deﬁne points and linear segments inside a stretch.
==PAGE:88==
76
Solution Architecture
stretch 0 <. "l1" .> 1
The above expression deﬁnes the two points {0, 1}, the linear segment (0, ”l1”, 1),
the connections Cleft = {(0, ”l1”)} and Cright = {(1, ”l1”)}.
stretch 0 <. "l1" .> 1 <. "l2" .> 2
These expression can be chained, e.g. the above expression deﬁnes the points
{0, 1, 2}, the linear segments {(0, ”l1”, 1), (1, ”l2”, 2)}, the connections Cleft =
{(0, ”l1”), (1, ”l2”)} and Cright = {(1, ”l1”), (2, ”l2”)}.
stretch 0 <*. "l1" .> 1 <. "l2" .*> 2
The expressions can also be deﬁned with the arrow operators containing a star,
which also deﬁnes a signal in the given direction. For example the above expres-
sion deﬁnes the points {0, 1, 2}, the linear segments {(0, ”l1”, 1), (1, ”l2”, 2)}, the
signals {(”l1”, 0), (”l2”, 2)}, the connections Cleft = {(0, ”l1”), (1, ”l2”)} and
Cright = {(1, ”l1”), (2, ”l2”)}.
In order to deﬁne junctions, several points are needed. As such the stretches
as their monadic values carry the leftmost and rightmost points of the stretch
meaning an expression of the form:
(p0,p2) <- stretch 0 <*. "l1" .> 1 <. "l2" .*> 2
Is identical to the previous expression except the point 0 has been bound to
the name p0 and the point 2 to p2. Junctions are deﬁned as junc :: label −→
point −→point −→point −→DSL, the order of the points is (pstem, pdown, pup).
Creating a junction also creates the relevant connections to this junction. As
such several stretches can be deﬁned and the end points used to create relevant
junctions. Below is an example of 3 linear segments all connection to a junction:
==PAGE:89==
5.5 Railway Game Construction
77
Figure 5.12: DSL: Example Railway
(_,j1s) <- stretch 0 <. "l1" .> 1
(j1d,_) <- stretch 2 <. "l2" .> 3
(jiu,_) <- stretch 4 <. "l3" .> 5
junc "j1" j1s j1d j1u
There are two way to deﬁne trains as shown below:
train "train1" LeftToRight (0,"l1",1) (1,"l2",3)
trainLabel "train1" LeftToRight "l1" "l2"
Both expressions deﬁne the same train named train1. The train is moving from
left to right, starts at the linear segment (0, ”l1”, 1) and has (1, ”l2”, 3) as des-
tination. Both expressions assume that the start and destination positions are
already deﬁned. The second expression is a shorthand which ﬁnds the linear
segment based on the label.
Figure 5.12 shows a simple example of a railway game. This game could be
deﬁned in the DSL as:
==PAGE:90==
78
Solution Architecture
exampleRailwayGame1 :: DSL String Int String ()
exampleRailwayGame1 = do
(_,j1s) <- stretch 0 <*. "l21" .*> 1 <. "l22" .> 2 <.
"l23" .> 3
,→
(j1u,_) <- stretch 4 <. "l11" .> 5 <. "l12" .*> 6
(j1d,_) <- stretch 7 <. "l24" .> 8 <. "l25" .> 9
junc "j1" j1s j1d j1u
trainLabel "train-blue" LeftToRight "l21" "l12"
The generic version of the signature is DSL label point train a and represents a
computation which results in a railway game. The type parameters label, point,
train represents the type of these concepts. The type a represents the monadic
value which is carried, in this case there is none, as only the stretches and arrow
operators carries a value.
The language elements always represent a computation which deﬁnes part of
a railway game. The example exampleRailwayGame1 as such is a new local
language element which in this case happens to deﬁne the entire game. This
need not be the case, for example it could be convenient to separate the deﬁni-
tion of the railway network, from the game deﬁnition such that the network can
be deﬁned once and several games built on top of it. Because of the monadic
deﬁnition of DSL this is possible. The example below also represents ﬁgure 5.12
but this time the model and game has been separated.
exampleRailwayModel :: DSL String Int String ()
exampleRailwayModel = do
(_,j1s) <- stretch 0 <*. "l21" .*> 1 <. "l22" .> 2 <.
"l23" .> 3
,→
(j1u,_) <- stretch 4 <. "l11" .> 5 <. "l12" .*> 6
(j1d,_) <- stretch 7 <. "l24" .> 8 <. "l25" .> 9
junc "j1" j1s j1d j1u
exampleRailwayGame2 :: DSL String Int String ()
exampleRailwayGame2 = do
exampleRailwayModel
trainLabel "train-blue" LeftToRight "l21" "l12"
==PAGE:91==
5.5 Railway Game Construction
79
Figure 5.13: Example of Junction Pattern
The value exampleRailwayModel represents the railway network, while exam-
pleRailwayGame2 represents a game conﬁguration built on top of exampleRail-
wayGame2. As such exampleRailwayGame1 = exampleRailwayGame2. An-
other game could be built on top of the exampleRailwayModel railway without
aﬀecting other game deﬁnitions. The railway game could be separated in diﬀer-
ent ways if desired.
Because the language is built on top of Haskell, all the features provided by
Haskell itself are still present while deﬁning railway games. One implicit fea-
ture which is potentially very useful the ability to deﬁne functions which creates
DSL fragments. These could be created if a common pattern in a railway is
detected. As such, rather than manually creating the full railway, the common
pattern could be parameterized and as such captured in a function.
Figure
5.13 represents a pattern which is quite common the railway models used in this
project. It represents two junctions where the stem points are facing in diﬀerent
direction. The up point of one of the junctions and the down point of another
are connected to a linear segment. As such a function junc2 is created which
captures this pattern, meaning a stretch and two junc elements in this common
conﬁguration can be created via a single junc2.
Other features are added. Some are deﬁned as patterns over other elements,
while others cannot be deﬁned purely based primitives as they need to manip-
ulate the underlying state. Figure 5.14 shows the same railway model as ﬁgure
5.12 except the model is used twice where one of the sub-models are ﬂipped.
The deﬁnition of this model is shown below:
==PAGE:92==
80
Solution Architecture
Figure 5.14: DSL: Example Railway Double
exampleModelDouble :: DSL (Int,String) (Int,Int) String ()
exampleModelDouble = do
importDSL 1 exampleRailwayModel
importDSL 2 (exampleRailwayModel >> flipDSL)
link (1,6) (12,"link6") (2,6)
linkDup 9 1 2 (12,"link9")
In this deﬁnition some new language elements are introduced.
First is im-
portDSL. This is a function which receives a DSL and a namespace and creates
a new version of that DSL where every label and point is preﬁxed with the
namespace, by creating a tuple consisting of namespace and the original ele-
ment. Due to this the signature of the DSL has changed.
The second new element is ﬂipDSL which receives a DSL and creates a version
where every element is ﬂipped. Both of these elements are deﬁned with special
knowledge of the underlying state.
Next is the link element. This is a function which creates a linear segment be-
tween two points. The main diﬀerence between this and stretch is; that stretch
==PAGE:93==
5.5 Railway Game Construction
81
deﬁnes both points and linear segments, while link assumes that the point are
already deﬁned. This element is also deﬁned with special knowledge.
The last element is linkDup. This is a variation of link which links duplicate
points in two namespaces. This element does not require special knowledge and
is simply a slight variation of link.
In the code exampleRailwayModel is already assumed to be deﬁned as shown
earlier. Initially exampleRailwayModel is imported into namespace 1. A new
version of exampleRailwayModel is ﬂipped and imported into namespace 2. next
the point 6 in namespace 1 is linked to point 6 in namespace 2. The resulting
linear segment is labelled ”link6” in the arbitrary new namespace 12. Lastly
the same is done with point 9 using the linkDup variation.
Finally there is a special function generateGame which receives a DSL and
generates: RailwayDeﬁnition, RailwayGame and initial game state.
5.5.2
On the Fly Validation
The DSL structure itself represents a computation which when provided to gen-
erateGame, creates the game itself. One advantage of representing each language
element as a state delta is that while generating the model the element itself re-
ceives the state which is deﬁned up to that point. The element can the validate
itself based in this partial model. There are several validations which could be
useful when creating the railway model. One is making sure that railway ele-
ments, and labels are not accidentally deﬁned multiple times. Other validations
would be to check if there are any cycles in the railway network or that the
game is well formed. An example of a non well formed game is if multiple trains
have the same destination, as this would never result in a valid plan.
In the current implementation the only validation, is checking if any elements or
labels are deﬁned multiple times and that all elements are deﬁned before they
are used in other deﬁnitions. This is in order to prevent confusing behaviour
caused by typos or other small mistakes. Checking the well-formedness of the
game would be quite easy as all the required information is present, but has not
been implemented. This has been de-prioritised as the train deﬁnitions them-
selves tend to represent a small fraction of the DSL deﬁnition. More complex
validation like cycle checks are not implemented as this would require more ad-
==PAGE:94==
82
Solution Architecture
vanced processing of the state which was not been deemed of high priority in
this project.
5.5.3
Trade-Oﬀs Relative to Parsed Language
As mentioned earlier the DSL is deﬁned inside the Haskell language. This has
some advantages and disadvantages. The main advantage, is that the entire
Haskell language infrastructure is available for free, making the language quite
strong without dedicating time to reinventing function deﬁnitions, type checking
and other common language features.
One minor downside is that the DLS syntax will naturally be somewhat limited
as the language still needs to be valid Haskell. Some names and symbols already
have pre-existing meanings in Haskell again limiting some of the deﬁnition the
DSL.
A more involved downside of building the DSL on top of Haskell is that it is
primarily a compiled language. As such it is not enough to simply deﬁne the
railway game in a text ﬁle and provide it as input to the program. Assuming
that the user does not wish to compile the entire implementation themselves it
leaves little option for separating the railway deﬁnitions from the solver:
The solver could be provided as a pre-compiled library.
The user will then
need to create a new Haskell program which contain the railway deﬁnitions.
In order to solve these games the program will need to invoke the solver with
these deﬁnitions.
In order to actually execute the program two options are
available. Either compile it normally, or use Haskell GHCi which is capable
of interpreting code, if the slowdown associated with the interpreted version is
deemed acceptable.
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 6
Implementation
Due to the heavy usage of HAMT’s, which are hash based structures, hash val-
ues are pre-calculated and cached along the original values. This is because
otherwise a disproportionate amount of time would be used, calculating hash
values. This is achieved via the type Hashed a which represents a tuple con-
taining the value and the hash. The types Linear label point and Switch label
point represent synonyms of Hashed (LinearSegment label point) and Hashed
(SwitchSegment label point). The type PointT point is a synonym for Hashed
point and the type GState label point train is a synonym for Hashed (GameState
label point train). The implementation code always uses these synonymous ver-
sions of the constructs for the sake of eﬃciency, the operations which is deﬁned
on the original types are also deﬁned for the synonym versions, meaning they
can be assumed to be identical. The original un-hashed versions of these four
structures are implemented exactly as described in the architecture. Similarly
RailwayDeﬁnition and GameDeﬁnition are implemented as described in the ar-
chitecture.
Some code artefacts have constraints like Label, Point and Train on the cor-
responding type parameters.
These are collections of constraints which are
required if the type is to be valid throughout the entire implementation. The
most important constraints are that they should deﬁne form total orders, and
be hashable, which is required by the usage of HAMT’s and other structures.
==PAGE:96==
84
Implementation
Other constraints used are Railway label point train m and Stack (GState la-
bel point train) label point train m these two are for all intents and purposes
identical. The second one is a relic from a previous implementation attempt.
Both of state that the type parameter m should be a monad which implements
the interface for the railway monad, which is to say have access to StaticData.
These constraints also add the previous mentioned constraints: Label, Point
and Train. This is done as to separate the actual implementation of the railway
monad, from its interface.
6.1
LiuSmolka
The LiuSmolka algorithm is implemented in a somewhat similar manner to the
local2 version shown in [LS98]. The main diﬀerence is that the error corrections
and optimisations described in the sections 2.2.4 and 2.2.5 are implemented.
Another aspect is that the implementation is deﬁned functionally.
W and the elements of D are implemented as functional lists. When concate-
nating elements onto these lists, the new elements are always added on the left
hand side of the (++) operator. This serves two purposes. The result is always
that new elements are added to the front. Because elements are also removed
from the front, this results in the behaviour of a stack. Another aspect is the
complexity involved. Because of non-strict evaluation, adding a list of elements
to the front results in a deferred calculation. When removing the front element,
the complexity is still O(1). This complexity can only be achieved if the (++)
operator is applied in a right associative manner, which this behaviour is.
==PAGE:97==
6.1 LiuSmolka
85
type HyperEdge vertex = (vertex, [vertex])
type Fixpoint vertex = Map.HashMap vertex Bool
type EdgeDep vertex = Map.HashMap vertex [HyperEdge vertex]
data FixpointValue = Zero | Unknown
deriving(Eq,Ord,Show,Generic)
instance Hashable FixpointValue where
data LiuSmolkaState vertex = LiuSmolkaState{
w :: [HyperEdge vertex],
a :: Fixpoint vertex,
d :: EdgeDep vertex
}
type WithInitVertexAndHyperEdgeFuncT vertex m =
ReaderT (vertex, vertex -> m [[vertex]]) m
type WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m =
StateT (LiuSmolkaLocal vertex)
(WithInitVertexAndHyperEdgeFuncT vertex m)
,→
The main types used by the implementation is shown above. The type vertex is
generic and represents the type of vertices which are used. The type LiuSmolka-
State is used to contain the global LiuSmolka values. HyperEdge, Fixpoint and
EdgeDep are type synonyms used for the sake of brevity. As described in section
5.3 two layers of monad is used. WithInitVertexAndHyperEdgeFuncT represents
a reader monad which lays on top of an arbitrary monad m, and contains the
hyper-edge function and initial vertex. The WithLiuSmolkaT monad is a state
monad containing a state of type LiuSmolkaState.
Next is a description of the implementation itself, the auxiliary functions are
described ﬁrst in the order they are used. Finally the ﬁnal composition is pre-
sented. It should be noted that the values in the ﬁxed-point assignment are
Booleans, rather than {0, 1}. The value ⊥is represented as a missing entry.
==PAGE:98==
86
Implementation
initLiuSmolka :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) => WithLiuSmolkaT
vertex m ()
,→
initLiuSmolka = do
v0 <- getInitVertex
e0 <- expandHyperEdges v0
put $ LiuSmolkaState{
w = e0,
a = Map.fromList [(v0,False)],
d = Map.empty
}
The function initLiuSmolka is used to create the initial instance of LiuSmolkaS-
tate. Like the abstract algorithm, the initial vertex is set to 0 in the assignment.
All hyper-edges with the initial vertex as source, are expanded and initialised
as W. The dependencies are initially empty.
popW :: (Monad m) => WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m (Maybe
(HyperEdge vertex))
,→
popW = do
liu@LiuSmolkaState{w=w} <- get
case w of
[] -> return Nothing
(h:t) -> do
put $ liu{w=t}
return $ Just h
The function popW is used to remove the next HyperEdge from W. The Hy-
perEdge is returned inside the monad. If W is empty the result is Nothing.
==PAGE:99==
6.1 LiuSmolka
87
data FixpointValue = Zero | Unknown
filterDestList :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
Fixpoint vertex ->
[vertex] ->
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m (Maybe (FixpointValue, vertex,
[vertex]))
,→
filterDestList _ [] = return Nothing
filterDestList a (h:t) = do
case Map.lookup h a of
Nothing -> return $ Just (Unknown, h, t)
Just False -> return $ Just (Zero, h, t)
Just True -> filterDestList a t
The auxiliary type FixpointValue is introduced. It represents the possible values
{0, ⊥} and is only used internally. The function ﬁlterDestList is used to repre-
sent the inner case statement of the LiuSmolka algorithm. It checks if A(v) = 1
for all destination vertices in a hyper-edge. If it is True then the value Nothing
is returned. Otherwise it ﬁnds the ﬁrst vertex where A(v) ̸= 1. It then deter-
mines if the value is 0 or ⊥. The function returns a FixpointValue representing
0 or ⊥, the vertex in case, and the remainder of the vertex list, which has yet
to be checked.
==PAGE:100==
88
Implementation
addToFixpoint :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) => vertex ->
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m ()
,→
addToFixpoint v = do
liu@LiuSmolkaState{w=w,d=d,a=a} <- get
initV <- getInitVertex
let aNext = Map.insert v True a
if v == initV then
put $ liu{
a = aNext,
w = []
}
else
put $ liu{
a = aNext,
w = maybe w (++ w) $ Map.lookup v d,
d = Map.delete v d
}
The function addToFixpoint represents the ﬁrst case branch, where every desti-
nation vertex is assigned 1 and as such the source vertex should also be assigned
to 1. As such the function simply sets the source vertex. Additionally the func-
tion also checks if the source vertex was the initial vertex. If it was the initial
vertex then W is emptied in order to stop the algorithm as a solution is found.
If the vertex was not the initial vertex, then then D(v) is added to W. One
small addition not described before is that D(v) is emptied once the assignment
A(v) := 1 has occurred.
This is possible because D(v) is never used again.
Removing it frees the memory used to represent the list and any non-evaluated
computations bound to this value.
addDependency :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
vertex ->
HyperEdge vertex ->
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m ()
addDependency v e = do
liu@LiuSmolkaState{d=d} <- get
put $ liu{
d = Map.insertWith (++) v [e] d
}
The function addDependency represents the second case branch. A destination
==PAGE:101==
6.1 LiuSmolka
89
vertex has been found such that A(v′) = 0. The function receives the vertex in
question v′ and the HyperEdge e and adds the hyper-edge e to D(v′).
expandGraph :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
vertex ->
HyperEdge vertex ->
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m ()
expandGraph v e = do
liu@LiuSmolkaState{w=w,d=d,a=a} <- get
nextE <- expandHyperEdges v
put $ liu{
a = Map.insert v False a,
d = Map.insertWith (++) v [e] d,
w = (nextE) ++ w
}
The function expandGraph represents the third case branch. A vertex has been
found such that A(v′) = ⊥. As such the vertex is assigned such that A(v′) = 0,
the HyperEdge is added to D(v′) like in addDependency and v′ is expanded and
the resulting HyperEdge are added to W.
liuSmolkaNext :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
HyperEdge vertex ->
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m ()
liuSmolkaNext e@(v,s) = do
a <- gets a
if (Map.lookup v a) == Just True then
return ()
else do
res <- filterDestList a s
case res of
Nothing -> addToFixpoint v
Just (Zero,hd,tl) -> addDependency hd (v,tl)
Just (Unknown,hd,tl) -> expandGraph hd (v,tl)
The function liuSmolkaNext represents a single iteration of the LiuSmolka loop,
and receives a HyperEdge. If A(v) = 1 for the source vertex, then the loop ends
without performing any action. Otherwise ﬁlterDestList is invoked in order to
==PAGE:102==
90
Implementation
determine which case branch to take. If the result is Nothing then A(v′) = 1
for all destination vertices and branch one is taken, invoking addToFixpoint.
If the A(v′) = 0 for the ﬁrst vertex where A(v′) ̸= 1, then the second branch
addDependency is invoked. And ﬁnally if A(v′) = ⊥then the third branch ex-
pandGraph is invoked. When invoking addDependency and expandGraph, the
remaining unchecked destination vertices of the current HyperEdge are com-
bined into a HyperEdge and provided to the branch. This ensures that each
normal edge is only processed once.
liuSmolkaLocal' :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
WithLiuSmolkaT vertex m ()
,→
liuSmolkaLocal' = do
me <- popW
a <- gets a
case me of
Nothing -> return ()
Just e -> do
liuSmolkaNext e
liuSmolkaLocal'
The function liuSmolkaLocal’ represents the entire LiuSmolka loop. It invokes
popW to get the next HyperEdge. If there is no more HyperEdge’s the function
terminates. Otherwise liuSmolkaNext is invoked, producing the next LiuSmolka-
State, followed by a recursive invocation of liuSmolkaLocal’.
liuSmolkaLocalT :: (Vertex vertex, Monad m) =>
(vertex -> m [[vertex]]) ->
vertex ->
m (vertex, Fixpoint vertex)
liuSmolkaLocalT expander v0 =
let withLiu = (initLiuSmolka >> liuSmolkaLocal' >>
getInitVertex) in
,→
let withReader = runStateT withLiu emptyLiuSmolka in
let state = runLiuReaderT withReader expander v0 in
fmap (mapSnd a) state
Finally the function liuSmolkaLocalT represents the entire implementation of
LiuSmolka local. Receives the HyperEdge function and initial vertex. It invokes
initLiuSmolka to initialise the state. It runs the computation inside the LiuS-
==PAGE:103==
6.2 Succesor Construction
91
molka monads and returns the ﬁxed-point assignment in the input monad. It
should be noted that the function also returns the initial vertex. At the moment
this is superﬂuous, and is simply a relic from an earlier version. The function
liuSmolkaLocalT is the implementation of the function liuSmolkaLocal described
in the architecture.
6.2
Succesor Construction
There are two general ways to derive the GState successors. One is the naive
version which creates every possible state. The other is the relevant successor
function which only ﬁnds relevant Controller state. This version also accepts a
Filter. and Priority.
6.2.1
Naive Controller Construction
deriveNewControllerState :: (Label label, Point point,
Train train) =>
,→
GState label point train ->
SignalSet label point ->
SwitchSet label point ->
GState label point train
deriveNewControllerState state signals switches =
cGState
Controller
signals
switches
(trainPositions state)
The function deriveNewControllerState is used to create a Controller state,
based on a predecessor Conductor state. The function receives the predecessor
game state, a set of green signals, a set of up switches, and returns a new game
state.
Given that Controller can manipulate signals and junctions, the new
state will have the same conﬁguration as the input. The player label of this
new state is always set to Controller. The train positions are copied from the
predecessor state and as such remains unchanged.
==PAGE:104==
92
Implementation
In order to create all possible Controller successor states some auxiliary func-
tions are needed.
type ControllerPermutations label point = [(SignalSet label
point, SwitchSet label point)]
,→
allPermutations :: (Foldable f, Eq a, Hashable a) => f a ->
[Set.HashSet a]
,→
allPermutations = fmap Set.fromList . subsequences . toList
controllerPermutations :: (Label label, Point point) =>
RailwayDefinition label point ->
ControllerPermutations label point
controllerPermutations rail = cartesianProductTuple
(allPermutations $ signals $ rail)
(allPermutations $ switchSegments $ rail)
The function allPermutations is used to create all possible sub-sequences of a
list, this is achieved by subsequences which is an inbuilt Haskell function. The
result is a list of lists which is transformed into a list of sets. Given that the
state of signals/junctions are deﬁned via membership of a set, this function will
create all possible conﬁgurations of a sequence of either signals or junctions. The
function controllerPermutations receives the RailwayDeﬁnition and creates all
possible Controller deltas. This is achieved by invoking allPermutations on the
junctions and signals found in the deﬁnition. The result is two lists of sets.
The cartesian product is then calculated for these two lists resulting in all pos-
sible combinations of signal and junction conﬁgurations. Because this is quite
an expensive computations it is only performed once and stored in the railway
monad, the computation is however lazy, meaning elements are only expanded
on demand. As such if this naive version of successor calculation is not used,
the entire list will remain un-evaluated.
==PAGE:105==
6.2 Succesor Construction
93
allControllerSuccessorStates :: (Railway label point train
m) =>
,→
GState label point train ->
m [GState label point train]
allControllerSuccessorStates state =
asksControllerPermutations $ fmap $ uncurry $
deriveNewControllerState state
,→
Finally the function allControllerSuccessorStates is used to calculate all possi-
ble Controller states from a Conductor state. This is achieved by reading the
result of controllerPermutations from the global monad. Each element in this
list represents the delta for a Controller state. As such the function deriveNew-
ControllerState is partially applied with the predecessor state, and subsequently
invoked on all Controller deltas.
6.2.2
Relevant Controller Construction
The algorithm for deriving the minimal set of relevant Controller states is de-
scribed in section 4.1 and involves ﬁnding all possible destinations for a train
while remembering what signals and junctions were encountered along the way.
data ReaderState label point train m = ReaderState{
gameState :: GState label point train,
train :: train,
connections :: ConnectionMap label point
}
data Fragment label point = Fragment{
fragGreenSignals :: SignalSet label point,
fragUpSwitches :: SwitchSet label point
}
type ControllerReaderT label point train m =
ReaderT (ReaderState label point train m) m
type WithFragmentT label point train m =
StateT (Fragment label point) (ControllerReaderT label
point train m)
,→
==PAGE:106==
94
Implementation
Initially a structure Fragment is created for storing signals and junctions en-
countered so far. by default these are empty. The computations are wrapped
in a state monad where the state is a Fragment. The global information used in
the computation is stored in a structure named ReaderState which is used by
a reader monad. The ReaderState contains the state from which the successors
are derived, the current train being traced and the connection map related to
the train direction.
In order to derive the states a set of visit functions are used to traverse the rail-
way. The return value of these functions are WithFragmentT label point train
m [(Linear label point, Fragment label point)]. Meaning a list of tuples, con-
taining a linear segment and a fragment inside the fragment monad. The linear
segment being the destination of the train found in the fragment monad, and
the fragment is the minimal conﬁguration needed for the train to arrive at that
destination.
visitPoint :: (Railway label point train m) =>
PointT point ->
WithFragmentT label point train m [(Linear label point,
Fragment label point)]
,→
visitPoint point = do
segOpt <- asksConnections (Map.lookup point)
case segOpt of
Nothing -> return []
Just seg -> visitSegment point seg
The function visitPoint receives a point, ﬁnds the next segment in the connec-
tion map and invokes visitSegment on the next segment. If no segment is found
then an empty list is produced, as moving the train would result in a derailment.
==PAGE:107==
6.2 Succesor Construction
95
visitSegment :: (Railway label point train m) =>
PointT point ->
Segment label point ->
WithFragmentT label point train m [(Linear label point,
Fragment label point)]
,→
visitSegment _ (LSegment linear) = visitLinear linear
visitSegment from (SSegment switch) = visitSwitch from
switch
,→
The functions visitSegment receives the point which the computation came from,
and invokes either visitLinear or visitSwitch based on what kind of segment it
was.
visitLinear :: (RailwayGameMonad label point train m) =>
Linear label point ->
WithFragmentT label point train m [(Linear label point,
Fragment label point)]
,→
visitLinear linear = do
frag <- get
return [(linear,frag)]
The function visitLinear receives the previous point and a linear segment. A
singleton list is returned containing the linear segment and the current fragment
from the fragment monad as a destination has been found.
==PAGE:108==
96
Implementation
visitSwitch :: (Railway label point train m) =>
PointT point ->
Switch label point ->
WithFragmentT label point train m [(Linear label point,
Fragment label point)]
,→
visitSwitch from switch
| isDownPoint switch from = visitPoint $ stemPoint switch
| isUpPoint switch from = addSwitch switch >> (visitPoint
$ stemPoint switch)
,→
| isStemPoint switch from = do
frag <- get
without <- lift $ evalStateT (visitPoint $ downPoint
switch) frag
,→
with <- lift $ evalStateT ((addSwitch switch) >>
(visitPoint $ upPoint switch)) frag
,→
return (without ++ with)
The function visitSwitch receives the previous point and a junction. It initially
checks if the previous point is up, down or stem. If the point is down, then
visitPoint is invoked on stem, without modifying the fragment, as down is the
default conﬁguration. If the point is up, then visitPoint is invoked on stem,
and the current junction is added to the fragment. If the point is stem, then it
initially visitPoint is invoked on down, without modifying the fragment. Next
visitPoint is invoked on up, with the current junction added. Finally the two
results are concatenated.
trainFragments :: (Railway label point train m) =>
GState label point train ->
train ->
m [(train, Linear label point, Fragment label point)]
trainFragments state train = do
let trainPos = validTrainPosition state train
point <- linearPointFromTrain train trainPos
res <- runFragStack ((addLinearExit trainPos point) >>
(visitPoint point)) state train
,→
return $ fmap (\(l,f) -> (train,l,f)) res
The function trainFragments is used to calculate all successor state deltas for a
single train. The function calculates the next point and invokes addLinearExit
==PAGE:109==
6.2 Succesor Construction
97
which checks if the current linear segment and next point, represents a signal.
If it is a signal then it is added to the fragment. Next visitPoint is invoked on
the next point. Because the result of visitPoint is inside the fragment monad,
the function runFragStack is invoked, unwrapping the two monads deﬁned by
the computation. The current train is then added to all results in the list.
stateFragments :: (Railway label point train m) =>
GState label point train ->
m [(train, Linear label point, Fragment label point)]
stateFragments state = do
trains <- asksGame trains
catMonadLists $ fmap (trainFragments state) trains
relevantControllerSuccessorStatesWithFilter :: (Ord k,
Railway label point train m) =>
,→
(GState label point train -> m Bool) ->
(GState label point train -> Linear label point -> train
-> m k) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m [GState label point train]
relevantControllerSuccessorStatesWithFilter predicate
ordering state = do
,→
frags <- stateFragments state
let toState = fragmentToControllerState state
prioFrag <- prioritizeFragments (ordering state) frags
let allStates = fmap (toState . trd3) prioFrag
filterM predicate allStates
The function stateFragments invokes trainFragments on all train and collects
the result. Finally the function relevantControllerSuccessorStatesWithFilter is
the top function used to derive all Controller states. stateFragments is invoked
resulting in all state deltas. These are sorted based on the input Priority and
ﬁnally the fragment of each element is turned into a Controller state, by invoking
deriveNewControllerState on the two sets from the fragment.
These GState
successors are then ﬁltered according to the input Filter.

6.2.3
Conductor Construction
deriveNewConductorState :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
GState label point train ->
train ->
GamePosition label point ->
GState label point train
deriveNewConductorState state train pos =
cGState
Conductor
(greenSignals state)
(upSwitches state)
(Map.insert train pos $ trainPositions state)
The function deriveNewConductorState is used to create a Conductor state from
a Controller predecessor, a train and a game position. Given that Conductor
can only move a single train, the new state is created by replacing the position
of the input train, with the input game position. The signal and junction sets
are copied from the predecessor.
In order to create the Conductor successor states, the possible path of all trains
are traced until the next linear segment is reached, or the train derails. If the
train cannot move, because of a red signal no successor is created.

The function ﬁndNextTrainDestinationFromSegment is used to ﬁnd the next
train position from a possible segment. If there is no segment, then the train
derails. If the segment is linear, then that is the trains next position. If the
segment is a junction then the exit point is found. This is done by following
the junction via the conﬁgured path. If the train is attempting to move into up
wile the junction is conﬁgured to down or vise verse, then the train derails.

findNextTrainDestination :: (Railway label point train m)

findNextTrainDestination state train = do

let exitPoint = getDirectionPoint trainPos trainDir

return Nothing
else do

nextSeg trainDir exitPoint
,→
The function ﬁndNextTrainDestination is used to ﬁnd the next possible destina-

ﬁndNextTrainDestinationFromSegment is invoked.

Finally are the functions combining the Controller and Conductor successor

allSuccessorStates :: (Railway label point train m) =>
GState label point train ->

The function allSuccessorStates is the naive successor function. It receives an
input state and based on who produced that state, the corresponding successor
function is invoked. If the previous player was Controller the successors are cal-

The function relevantSuccessorStates is the optimised version and receives a

6.3 Floyd-Warshall
103
Stopping the successor construction is addressed in section 6.7.
6.3
Floyd-Warshall
The Floyd-Warshall implementation, is split into two parts.
The algorithm
itself is implemented independently from the domain, and is fairly similar to
the abstract version of the algorithm. The other aspect is the transformation of
the railway network, into a directed graph on which Floyd-Warshall can operate.
6.3.1
Construction
In the abstract deﬁnition of Floyd-Warshall, the result is stored in a 2-dimensional
list structure. If two indices are not transitively connected the special value ∞
is stored. In this implementation the result is stored as mapping from a tuple of
vertices to a weight, missing connections are simply not stored. The implemen-
tation is parameterised over both vertex type and weight type. The vertex type
needs to deﬁne equality and hashing, in order to be used as keys. The value
type needs to form a total order, and needs to deﬁne basic arithmetic operators.
When working with values which may be ∞then the value is wrapped in the
Maybe type. In this context the value Nothing is synonymous to ∞.
cartesianProduct3 :: (a -> b -> c -> d) -> [a] -> [b] ->
[c] -> [d]
,→
cartesianProduct3 f as bs cs = do
a <- as
b <- bs
fmap (f a b) cs
cartesianProductTuple3 :: [a] -> [b] -> [c] -> [(a,b,c)]
cartesianProductTuple3 = cartesianProduct3 (,,)
enumerateVertexPermutations :: [vertex] -> [(vertex,
vertex, vertex)]
,→
enumerateVertexPermutations v = cartesianProductTuple3 v v
v
,→
==PAGE:116==
104
Implementation
The function cartesianProduct3 receives 3 lists and a function. It then lazily
creates a list which is the cartesian product of all elements in the 3 lists mapped
through the input function. cartesianProductTuple3 is a variant of cartesian-
Product3 where the input function is predeﬁned to create a triple. The function
enumerateVertexPermutations is used to create all triples of the input vertices,
and represents the triple nested loops in the Floyd-Warshall deﬁnition.
infMin :: (I i) => Maybe i -> Maybe i -> Maybe i
infMin mx Nothing = mx
infMin Nothing my = my
infMin (Just x) (Just y) = Just $ min x y
(?+?) :: (Num i) => Maybe i -> Maybe i -> Maybe i
(?+?) = liftA2 (+)
The function infMin is used as a minimum function for Maybe i, where i is the
weight type. It states that the special value Nothing is greater than all other
possible values. The inﬁx function (?+?) is used to deﬁne addition over Maybe
i. It states that Nothing plus any other value yields Nothing, otherwise it is
deﬁned as normal addition as deﬁned by the type i.
type FloydMap k v = Map.HashMap (k,k) v
addEdge :: (Vertex vertex, I i) => FloydMap vertex i ->
(vertex, vertex, i) -> FloydMap vertex i
,→
addEdge d (x,y,w) = Map.alter (infMin (Just w)) (x,y) d
expandVertex :: (I i, Monad m) => (vertex -> m
[(vertex,i)]) -> vertex -> m [(vertex,vertex,i)]
,→
expandVertex successorFunc v = do
succs <- successorFunc v
return $ (v,v,0) : fmap (\(y,w) -> (v,y,w)) succs
The type FloydMap k v is a synonym for HashMap (k,k) v, meaning a map from
a tuple of the vertex type to the weight type. The function addEdge receives
a weighted edge and inserts into the map. The function Map.alter is a special
function with the signature: (Maybe v −→Maybe v)−> k −→HashMap k v −→
HashMap k v. It receives a key, a value a map and a function. It can then
==PAGE:117==
6.3 Floyd-Warshall
105
either insert the key-value pair, remove the key or alter the value associated
with the key based on the input function [Tib]. The behaviour of this function
corresponds with the intended eﬀect of not storing ∞values. The function does
allow for multi-graphs, meaning there are several edges between two vertices
with the same direction and diﬀerent weights. In this case the lowest weight is
chosen. The function expandVertex receives a vertex and a successor function
and creates the corresponding edges, as a special case it also implicitly creates
a 0 cost edge to the vertex itself. The type m could be any monadic type. The
implementation does not make use of a monad internally, but it allows a monad
to be provided externally if needed.
recalculateDIJ :: (Vertex vertex, I i) => FloydMap vertex i
-> (vertex, vertex, vertex) -> FloydMap vertex i
,→
recalculateDIJ d (k,i,j) = Map.alter (infMin (dik ?+? dkj))
(i,j) d
,→
where
dik = Map.lookup (i,k) d
dkj = Map.lookup (k,j) d
The function recalculateDIJ represents the inner if statement of Floyd-Warshall.
The function does the following: dist[i][j] := min(dist[i][j], dist[i][k]+dist[k][j]).
The functions infMin and (?+?) are used for the minimum check and addition.
==PAGE:118==
106
Implementation
createInitD :: (Vertex vertex, I i) => [(vertex, vertex,
i)] -> FloydMap vertex i
,→
createInitD = foldl' addEdge Map.empty
floydWarshall' :: (Vertex vertex, I i) => FloydMap vertex i
-> [(vertex,vertex,vertex)] -> FloydMap vertex i
,→
floydWarshall' = foldl' recalculateDIJ
floydWarshall :: (Vertex vertex, I i, Monad m) => (vertex
-> m [(vertex,i)]) -> [vertex] -> m (FloydMap vertex i)
,→
floydWarshall successorFunc vs =
let kij = enumerateVertexPermutations vs in
let e = catMonadLists $ fmap (expandVertex successorFunc)
vs in
,→
let initD = liftM createInitD e in
liftM (`floydWarshall'` kij) initD
The function ﬂoydWarshall represents the entire Floyd-Warshall implementa-
tion.
It receives a graph and produces a map containing the shortest paths
between all vertices. In this case a graph is deﬁned as a list of vertices and a
successor function, which receives a vertex and produces a list of pairs contain-
ing the destination vertex and the weight of the edge.
The function initially creates the list kij representing all iterations of the nested
loops. It expands all vertices, creating a list of edges. It then creates the initial
distance map, using the function createInitD which adds all edges. Finally it
iterates through all triples in kij, invoking recalculateDIJ representing the loop.
As mentioned in section 4.2.1, the linear segments are treated as vertices when
run through Floyd-Warshall. In order to create the distance maps for the railway
network, a set of functions are created which represents the successor function
needed by ﬂoydWarshall.
==PAGE:119==
6.3 Floyd-Warshall
107
expandPoint :: (Point point) => ConnectionMap label point
-> PointT point -> [Linear label point]
,→
expandPoint connections point = case Map.lookup point
connections of
,→
Nothing -> []
Just (LSegment linear) -> [linear]
Just (SSegment switch) -> expandPointToSwitch connections
point switch
,→
expandSwitchPoints :: (Eq point) => PointT point -> Switch
label point -> [PointT point]
,→
expandSwitchPoints point switch
| isStemPoint switch point = [upPoint switch, downPoint
switch]
,→
| otherwise = [stemPoint switch]
expandPointToSwitch :: (Point point) =>
ConnectionMap label point ->
PointT point ->
Switch label point ->
[Linear label point]
expandPointToSwitch connections point switch =
concatMap (expandPoint connections) $ expandSwitchPoints
point switch
,→
The function expandPoint receives the connections used for a single direction in
the railway, and a point. If the next segment from this point is a linear segment,
then the successor is simply that linear segment. Otherwise if it is a junction,
then the function expandPointToSwitch is called. The function expandPoint-
ToSwitch receives a junction and the predecessor point and ﬁnds the successor
linear segments. Initially the function expandSwitchPoints is invoked, ﬁnding
the next points which is either stem, or up and down, based on the direction.
Each of these points are then provided to expandPoint again, and the ﬁnal result
in ﬂattened into a single list.
==PAGE:120==
108
Implementation
addWeight :: (Num i) => vertex -> (vertex, i)
addWeight v = (v,1)
expandRailwayEdges :: (Point point, I i) =>
RailwayDefinition label point ->
Direction ->
Linear label point ->
[(Linear label point, i)]
expandRailwayEdges rail dir linear = case dir of
RightToLeft -> fmap addWeight leftEdges
LeftToRight -> fmap addWeight rightEdges
where
leftEdges = expandPoint (rightToLeftConnections rail)
(leftPoint linear)
,→
rightEdges = expandPoint (leftToRightConnections rail)
(rightPoint linear)
,→
The combined successor function is expandRailwayEdges. This function receives
the railway network and a direction, resulting in a function conforming to the
expectation of ﬂoydWarshall. This function extracts the relevant connection
map from the network and invokes the expandPoint function by ﬁnding the cor-
rect point, from the input linear segment. All of the results are mapped through
addWeight, adding a constant weight of 1 to all edges.

6.3 Floyd-Warshall

type DirAPSP label point =

type APSP label point =

railwayFloydWarshall :: (Label label, Point point, Monad m)
=> RailwayDefinition label point -> m (APSP label
point)
,→
,→
railwayFloydWarshall rail = do
let floydFunc e = floydWarshall e $ toList $
linearSegments rail
,→
rightToLeft <- floydFunc $ (return . expandRailwayEdges
rail RightToLeft)
,→
leftToRight <- floydFunc $ (return . expandRailwayEdges
rail LeftToRight)
,→
return $ Map.fromList [(RightToLeft,rightToLeft),
(LeftToRight,leftToRight)]
,→
The type DirAPSP is a synonym corresponding to FloydMap where with the
substitutions {k −→Linear label point, v −→Int}, representing a distance map
for a single direction. The type APSP a synonym for a mapping from a Direc-
tion to DirAPSP, representing both distance maps for a railway. The function
railwayFloydWarshall calculates APSP based on the railway deﬁnition. The
function simply invokes ﬂoydWarshall for each direction, and maps the results
to the corresponding direction. All linear segments found in the railway is used
as vertices and expandRailwayEdges is used as the successor function. Given
that ﬂoydWarshall expects the successor function to return a monadic value,
expandRailwayEdges is wrapped in a monadic value m and as such the ﬁnal
result is given inside the m monad. This monad is again not deﬁned and and
be substituted for any monad.
==PAGE:122==
110
Implementation
6.3.2
Prioritisation & Filtration
lookupAPSP :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
train ->
Linear label point ->
Maybe Int
lookupAPSP game apsp train linear = Map.lookup
(linear,goal) dirAPSP
,→
where
(dir,_,goal) = trainDefinition game train
dirAPSP = apsp Map.! dir
filterLinear :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
train ->
Linear label point ->
Bool
filterLinear game apsp train linear = isJust $ lookupAPSP
game apsp train linear
,→
The function lookupAPSP receives a GameDeﬁniton, an APSP, a train and a
Linear. It proceeds to ﬁnd the direction and destination of the train, create a
tuple consisting of the input Linear and the train destination, and returning
the minimal distance from the input to the destination. If a path does not exist
the result is Nothing. The function ﬁlterLinear is a slightly modiﬁed version
which invokes lookupAPSP. If the result was Nothing then ﬁlterLinear returns
False, otherwise it returns True.
