The functions prioritizeShortestPath and ﬁlterThroughFloydWarshall are the
Priority and SubFilter versions of the two previous functions. The GameDeﬁni-
tion and APSP is retrieved from the railway monad, and the GState parameter
is added in order have the correct signature. prioritizeShortestPath returns the
largest possible integer if lookupAPSP returned Nothing, otherwise it returns
the same value. ﬁlterThroughFloydWarshall does not transform the output of
ﬁlterLinear.
6.4
Bottlenecks
The calculation of bottleneck concept is split into 3 parts. First is the generic
calculation of All-Common-Substrings. Second is the calculation of the overlap-
ping paths. And lastly is the generation and solution of small railway games in
order to ﬁnd the true bottlenecks.
==PAGE:124==
112
Implementation
6.4.1
All-Common-Substrings
The All-Common-Substrings implementation is based on the dynamic program-
ming implementation of Longest-Common-Substring. Initially the Longest-Common-
Substring algorithm is used to create a table of substrings. Afterwards the table
is traversed in reverse order, collecting all substrings which are found. Each
character position will only be part of the longest local substring. The imple-
mentation is 0-indexed. Here a string does not need to represent text, it can be
a string of any type which is equatable.
The implementation is based on string indices. In order to create strings of ar-
bitrary type with eﬃcient lookup, the Haskell ’Sequence’ library is used [FFPS],
which is based on ﬁnger trees [HP06].
type Table = Map.HashMap (Int,Int) Int
data SubStringTableState = SubStringTableState {
l :: Table,
z :: Int
}
The type Table represents the dynamic programming table which is generated.
It is represented as a map between a pair of integers representing string indices,
to an integer representing the length of the common substring at that index pair.
The type SubStringTableState is used to chain the state through the implemen-
tation, and could be used in a state monad. SubStringTableState contains the
current Table and the length of the longest common substring found so far.
nextLij :: (Int,Int) -> Table -> Table
nextLij ij@(0,_) l = Map.insert ij 1 l
nextLij ij@(_,0) l = Map.insert ij 1 l
nextLij ij@(i,j) l =
let prevLij = maybeToInt $ Map.lookup (i-1,j-1) l in
Map.insert ij (prevLij + 1) l
The function nextLij represents the inner branch of the nested loop, which is
invoked if the current index pair represents the two equal characters. The two
==PAGE:125==
6.4 Bottlenecks
113
ﬁrst branches means that the one of the characters is the head of a string and
as such not part of an existing substring. A new substring is created of length
1. The third branch means that the none of the indices are head. The length
of the previous substring is found, which is diagonal up. The current substring
as such is one larger than the previous one.
nextState :: (Eq a) =>
Seq.Seq a ->
Seq.Seq a ->
SubStringTableState ->
(Int,Int) ->
SubStringTableState
nextState s t state@SubStringTableState{l=l,z=z} ij@(i,j)
| (Seq.index s i) == (Seq.index t j) =
let lij = maybeToInt $ Map.lookup ij l in
state {l=nextLij ij l, z = nextZ z lij}
| otherwise = state
substrTable :: (Eq a) => Seq.Seq a -> Seq.Seq a -> Table
substrTable s t = l $ foldl' (nextState s t)
emptyTableState (ijs s t)
,→
The function nextState represents the entirety of the inner nested loop. It re-
ceives the two strings and index pairs. If the indices represents characters which
are equal, then nextLij is invoked and z, which is the length of the longest sub-
string, is updated if the new common substring is longer. If the two characters
are not equal nothing is changed. This is because missing table entries are in-
terpreted as being 0. Meaning there is no reason to store these entries.
The function substrTable calculates the entire table of common substrings. This
is done by creating a list of all index pairs, and folding over them using nextState.
The table is then extracted from the ﬁnal SubStringTableState.
The next part is to collect the results found in the Table, in order to create the
common substrings.
==PAGE:126==
114
Implementation
data SubStringCollectState = SubStringCollectState {
collected :: Set.HashSet (Int,Int)
}
The type SubStringCollectState is used to contain a set of indices which have
been collected so far. This is to ensure that the same index pair is not collected
into multiple strings.
continueDiagonal :: Table -> (Int,Int) -> Bool
continueDiagonal l ij =
case maybeToInt $ Map.lookup ij l of
i | i <= 0 -> False
_ -> True
collectDiagonal ::
Table ->
([Int],SubStringCollectState) ->
(Int,Int) ->
([Int],SubStringCollectState)
collectDiagonal l (acc,state) ij@(i,j) =
if continueDiagonal l ij then
let ijNext = (i-1,j-1) in
let stateNext = addCollected ij state in
collectDiagonal l ((i : acc),stateNext) ijNext
else
(acc,state)
The functions continueDiagonal and collectDiagonal are used to collect a single
common substring. Once collectDiagonal is invoked then a substring or part
of a substring has been encountered for the ﬁrst time, the function receives a
list of integers, representing the current string being collected. The integer is
the index of the characters, in one of the strings. Initially continueDiagonal is
invoked which checks that the current index pair is not 0/empty in the table. If
the current pair is 0/empty, then the current collected string is returned. Other-
wise the current character is added to the current common substring, the pair is
added to SubStringCollectState and collectDiagonal is invoked for the diagonal
up element.
==PAGE:127==
6.4 Bottlenecks
115
collectCell ::
Table ->
([[Int]],SubStringCollectState) ->
(Int,Int) ->
([[Int]],SubStringCollectState)
collectCell l as@(acc,state) ij =
case filterTableEntry $ Map.lookup ij l of
False -> as
True -> case filterCollected ij state of
False -> as
True ->
let (accNext,stateNext) = collectDiagonal l
([],state) ij in
,→
(accNext : acc,stateNext)
The function collectCell is used to collect the common substring from an index
pair, assuming that it is not part of an already collectd common substring. ﬁl-
terTableEntry is used to check if the current pair larger than 0 in the Table. If
it is not then there is no substring and nothing occurs. If the current pair does
represent a common substring then ﬁlterCollected is used to check if it is already
collected. If it is already collected nothing occurs, otherwise collectDiagonal is
invoked for the pair, collection the entire common substring.
subStrCollect :: Seq.Seq a -> Seq.Seq a -> Table -> [[a]]
subStrCollect s t l =
fmap (fmap (Seq.index s)) $
fst $
foldl' (collectCell l) ([],emptyCollectState) $ ijsRev s
t
,→
allCommonSubStrings :: (Eq a) => Seq.Seq a -> Seq.Seq a ->
[[a]]
,→
allCommonSubStrings s t = subStrCollect s t $ substrTable s
t
,→
The function subStrCollect is used to collect all common substring in a Table
is iterates through all index pairs in reverse order and invokes collectCell. The
function allCommonSubStrings is the combined function for ﬁnding all common
substrings. It initially invokes substrTable creating the Table. Next subStrCol-
==PAGE:128==
116
Implementation
lect is invoked, collecting the substrings. Finally the indices are looked up in the
original string, transforming the substrings from indices to the actual characters.
6.4.2
Construction
Here the construction of the overlapping paths/bottlenecks is described.
type Path label point = Seq.Seq (Linear label point)
type TrainPaths label point train =
Map.HashMap train [Path label point]
type Chocke label point = Set.HashSet (Linear label point)
type ChockePoints label point train =
Map.HashMap
(train,Linear label point)
(Map.HashMap train (Chocke label point))
The type Path represents a single valid path for a train, it is represented as
a sequence of Linear’s. The sequence type is used such that allCommonSub-
Strings can understand a path as a string. TrainPaths is a mapping between
a trains and all valid paths for that train. Chocke is a single overlapping path,
represented as a set for eﬃcient lookup. ChockePoints is a map from a train and
a linear segment, to another map. The inner map, maps a train to a Chocke.
A single entry (t1, l1) −→t2 −→c, means the train t1 has the entry point l1 into
the overlapping path c, and the opposite moving train which the path overlaps
with is t2.
==PAGE:129==
6.4 Bottlenecks
117
constructTrainPaths :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
TrainPaths label point train
constructTrainPaths rail game apsp =
let (tLeft,tRight) = (\(l,r) -> (fmap fst l, fmap fst r))
$ partition ((== LeftToRight) . snd) $
trainDirections game in
,→
,→
let lPaths = fmap (\t -> (t,constructPathsTrain rail game
apsp t)) tLeft in
,→
let rPaths = fmap (\t -> (t,fmap Seq.reverse $
constructPathsTrain rail game apsp t)) tRight in
,→
Map.fromList (lPaths ++ rPaths)
The function constructTrainPaths is used to calculate all valid paths for every
train. Initially all train are partitioned into two lists based on the direction
of the train. All paths are then calculated for each train, the paths for trains
facing RightToLeft are reversed such that the allCommonSubStrings can ﬁnd
common substrings. Finally all trains and their associated paths are collected
into a TrainPaths. The algorithm for ﬁnding all valid paths for a train is very
similar to how relevant successors are calculated. The diﬀerence being that the
algorithm stops once a goal has been reached and the path is retained.
==PAGE:130==
118
Implementation
pathEnds :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
(train,train) ->
[Linear label point] ->
((train,Linear label point), (train,Linear label point),
Chocke label point)
,→
pathEnds (tl,tr) path =
let (ll,lr) = findEnds path in
let chocke = Set.fromList path in
((tl,ll), (tr,lr), chocke)
trainOverlappingPaths :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
TrainPaths label point train ->
(train,train) ->
[((train,Linear label point), (train,Linear label point),
Chocke label point)]
,→
trainOverlappingPaths paths ts@(tl,tr) =
let pathProd = cartesianProductTuple (paths Map.! tl)
(paths Map.! tr) in
,→
let overlapPaths = concatMap (uncurry
allCommonSubStrings) pathProd in
,→
fmap (pathEnds ts) overlapPaths
The function trainOverlappingPaths receives a TrainPaths and a pair of opposite
facing trains, and calculates a list of overlapping paths for the train pair. Here
an overlapping path is represented as ((train,Linear label point), (train,Linear
label point), Chocke label point). The structure is a triple, the ﬁrst two elements
are pairs, matching a train with its entry segment. The last element represents
the overlapping path.
Initially all paths are looked up for both trains and the cartesain product of the
opposite facing paths is created. Each of these path pairs is feed into allCom-
monSubStrings resulting in a list of common sub-paths. The given that each
pair results in a list, this structure is ﬂattened into a single list. Finally the ends
of each common sub-path is found and associated with the train in question,
and the sub-path is turned into a set, via the function pathEnds.
==PAGE:131==
6.4 Bottlenecks
119
allChockes :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
[((train,Linear label point), (train,Linear label point),
Chocke label point)]
,→
allChockes rail game apsp =
let trainPairs = oppositeDirectionTrainPairs game in
let paths = constructTrainPaths rail game apsp in
concatMap (trainOverlappingPaths paths) trainPairs
The function allChockes creates all pairs of opposite facing trains and invokes
trainOverlappingPaths. The result of each train pair is ﬂattened into a single
list, resulting in all overlapping paths in the railway game.
listMapper :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
((train,Linear label point) -> (train,Linear label point)
-> Bool) ->
,→
((train,Linear label point), (train,Linear label point),
Chocke label point) ->
,→
[((train,Linear label point),(train,Chocke label point))]
listMapper filter x@(p1@(t1,l1),p2@(t2,l2),c) =
case filter p1 p2 of
False -> []
True -> [((t1,l1), (t2,c)), ((t2,l2), (t1,c))]
The function listMapper serves two purposes. It receives a ﬁltering function
which receives two pairs of trains and entrances. It then receives an overlapping
path. The two pairs from the overlapping path is feed into the ﬁlter. If the
ﬁlter returns False then the overlapping path is not retained and an empty list
is produced. Otherwise the overlapping path is transformed into two entries of
a ChockePoints.
==PAGE:132==
120
Implementation
constructChockes :: (Label label, Point point, Train train)
=>
,→
((train,Linear label point) -> (train,Linear label point)
-> Bool) ->
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
ChockePoints label point train
constructChockes filter rail game apsp =
let list = allChockes rail game apsp in
let mList = concatMap id $ withStrategy listStrategy $
fmap (listMapper filter) list in
,→
let mapList = fmap (mapSnd (Map.fromList . return)) mList
in
,→
Map.fromListWith Map.union $ mapList
The function constructChockes is used to create all a ChockePoints for a railway
game. The function also receives a ﬁlter. allChockes is invoked resulting in a
list of overlapping paths. Each path is then feed into listMapper together with
the ﬁlter. The result is ﬂattened and turned into a ChockePoints.
The worst case number of overlapping paths is potentially very large. As such
invoking listMapper on the overlapping paths is done in parallel. The function
(withStrategy listStrategy) is used to ﬁlter the overlapping paths in parallel. It
partitions the list of overlapping paths, into sub-lists of length 20.
Each of
these partitions are then transformed in parallel. The results is identical to the
sequential version, except every 20’th overlapping path spawns a new parallel
computation.
The number 20 is slightly arbitrary, and requires benchmark-
ing in order to determine the optimal number. If the number was 1, then every
overlapping path would spawn a parallel computation. Given that modern com-
puters tend to have 4-8 cores, this could result in a slowdown, as the computer
could never run all these tasks simultaneously. The other extreme would be
to partition the list into n chunks, where n is the number of cores. This may
however not be ideal, as the list is implemented as a single linked list, mean-
ing the time taken create these large partitions, could take too long before the
actual parallel process is spawned. The framework for deterministic parallelism
described in [MML+10] is used to archive this.
If the naive version of overlapping paths is used, then the ﬁlter provided to
constructChockes should always return True. In order to ﬁnd the true bottle-
==PAGE:133==
6.4 Bottlenecks
121
necks, the ﬁlter however should create and solve a small LiuSmolka game. The
following describes this process.
createGame :: (Train train) =>
GameDefinition label point train ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
GameDefinition label point train
createGame game (t1,l1) (t2,l2) =
let (d1,_,g1) = trainDefinition game t1 in
let (d2,_,g2) = trainDefinition game t2 in
GameDefinition{
trainDefinitions = Map.fromList [(t1,(d1,l1,g1)),
(t2,(d2,l2,g2))]
,→
}
The function createGame is used to create the LiuSmolka game, used to vali-
date an overlapping path. It receives the deﬁnition of the original game, and
the train-entrance pair for the two trains. The result is a new game, where
only the two train are present. Their destinations and direction are identical
to the original game, but their stating positions are changed to be equal to the
entrances to an overlapping path.
validateBottleneck :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
(RailwayDefinition label point -> GameDefinition label
point train -> APSP label point -> Maybe Bool) ->
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
Bool
validateBottleneck solFunc rail gameIn apsp e1 e2 =
let game = createGame gameIn e1 e2 in
case solFunc rail game apsp of
(Just True) -> False
(Just False) -> True
==PAGE:134==
122
Implementation
The function validateBottleneck receives the train-entrance pairs which identi-
ﬁes an overlapping path. It also receives the RailwayDeﬁnition, RailwayGame
and APSP, together with a function for solving a LiuSmolka game. The func-
tion then invokes the solver function and returns True if the solver did not ﬁnd
a solution.
bottleneckApp :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
DummyApp label point train (Maybe Bool)
bottleneckApp = do
game <- askGame
(v0,fix) <- liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint
(relevantSuccessorStates filterThroughFloydWarshall
prioritizeShortestPath) $ createInitialState game
,→
,→
return $ Map.lookup v0 fix
trueBottleneckFilter :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
(train,Linear label point) ->
Bool
trueBottleneckFilter rail game apsp e1 e2 =
validateBottleneck (runDummyAppBounded $ bottleneckApp)
rail game apsp e1 e2
,→
The function trueBottleneckFilter receives RailwayDeﬁntion, GameDeﬁnition
and APSP and results in a ﬁlter function used by constructChockes. It creates
a solver function by using liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint. The successor function is
predetermined to be relevantSuccessorStates, using APSP both as SubFilter and
priority. This has been chosen for the sake of simplicity while still remaining
fairly powerful. Other combinations of ﬁlters and priorities could have been
used instead. The value bottleneckApp represents the game inside the railway
monad. Instead of the full monad, a DummyApp has been used, where only the
required values in StaticData is deﬁned.
==PAGE:135==
6.4 Bottlenecks
123
constructTrueBottlenecks :: (Label label, Point point,
Train train) =>
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
ChockePoints label point train
constructTrueBottlenecks rail game apsp = constructChockes
(trueBottleneckFilter rail game apsp) rail game apsp
,→
Finally the function constructTrueBottlenecks represents the combined function
for calculating the true bottlenecks, by invoking constructChockes with the ﬁlter
trueBottleneckFilter.
When invoking this function the parallelism described
earlier is implicitly used. The result is that every 20’th LiuSmolka game is run
in parallel.
6.4.3
Prioritisation & Filtration
destInOccupiedChocke :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
ChockePoints label point train ->
GState label point train ->
Linear label point ->
train ->
Bool
destInOccupiedChocke chockes state linear train = fromMaybe
False $ do
,→
innerMap <- Map.lookup (train,linear) chockes
let trainPos = fmap (mapSnd forceGamePositionToPosition)
$ Map.toList $ trainPositions state
,→
let fTrainPos = filter (((/=) train) . fst) trainPos
return $ any (anyFilter innerMap) fTrainPos
where
anyFilter m (t,l) = fromMaybe False $ do
chocke <- Map.lookup t m
return $ Set.member l chocke
After ChockePoints has been constructed, it can be used in priorities and ﬁlters.
The function destInOccupiedChocke receives a ChockePoints, a GState, a train
==PAGE:136==
124
Implementation
and a Linear. It determines whether the train is about to enter an overlapping
path which is already occupied. This is done by initially using the input train
and linear segment as lookup key in ChockePoints. If no result was found then
there exists no overlapping paths for the train and the result if False. If a re-
sult is found, then the map from opposite moving trains to overlapping path
sets is received from ChockePoints. The function then checks for every train,
except the input train, if it has an entry in the inner map. If it does not then
the result is False. If it has an entry then the position of the current train is
checked for membership in the corresponding overlapping path. The result is
the disjunction of all trains checked through the inner map. As such if the input
train is in any overlapping path, with the corresponding opposite moving train
also present, the result is True.
If used as a SubFilter then the result is negated. If the train is entering an
occupied bottleneck then the result is False. It is important that only true bot-
tlenecks are used for this, otherwise a valid solution may be destroyed. If used
as a Priority then the result of destInOccupiedChocke is transformed into an
OccupationState using the rule {True −→Occupied, False −→Free}.
Assuming constant time lookup in a HAMT, then the function destInOccupied-
Chocke has a time complexity of O(|T|).
6.5
Uncontrollable Stretches
6.5.1
Construction
In order to construct the uncontrollable stretches, a traversal of the railway is
made. For each train the railway is traversed once, following all possible paths.
The traversal starts at the trains initial position, the stretches are however col-
lected bottom up.
Most of the functions used in the construction requires an instance of a type
called StaticData. It is very important to note that this type is entirely local
to the construction of uncontrollable stretches and diﬀerent from the similarly
named concept from chapter 5. StaticData is deﬁned as:
==PAGE:137==
6.5 Uncontrollable Stretches
125
data StaticData label point train = StaticData {
railway :: RailwayDefinition label point,
game :: GameDefinition label point train,
train :: train,
connections :: ConnectionMap label point,
apsp :: DirAPSP label point
}
StaticData represents the global data which is used throughout the construc-
tion. It contains the RailwayDeﬁnition, GameDeﬁnition, the current train, the
connections relevant for the direction of the train and the APSP information
relevant for the direction of the train.
Most of the functions returns a value of the type:
([Linear label point],[[Linear label point]])
This tuple represents the uncontrollable stretches. The ﬁrst member is the cur-
rent stretch which is being constructed, the second member is a list of stretches
which has been constructed, also called closed stretches. The code used for gen-
erating the paths is deﬁned as:
filterLinearSD :: (Label label, Point point, Train train)
=>
,→
Linear label point ->
StaticData label point train ->
Bool
filterLinearSD linear sd =
let goal = trainGoal (game sd) (train sd) in
isJust $ Map.lookup (linear,goal) (apsp sd)
The function ﬁlterLinearSD is used to detect when a linear segment is encoun-
tered which is known to result in an invalid path. Currently the function simply
checks if there exists a path from the linear segment to the designated goal via
the APSP information, it is this functions which allows for the detection of rule 4.
==PAGE:138==
126
Implementation
hasSignal :: (Label label, Point point) =>
Linear label point ->
PointT point ->
StaticData label point train ->
Bool
hasSignal linear point sd = linearExitHasSignal linear
point $ railway sd
,→
The function hasSignal is used to detect of a linear segment has a signal facing
in direction of the train. The function linearExitHasSignal is used to look up
if such a signal exists in RailwayDeﬁnition. It is this function which allows the
detection of rule 2.
nextSegment :: (Point point) =>
PointT point ->
StaticData label point train ->
Maybe (Segment label point)
nextSegment point = Map.lookup point . connections
The function nextSegment is an auxiliary function used to ﬁnd the next segment
from a point, this is achieved by performing a lookup in the connections in the
StaticData.
visitPoint :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
StaticData label point train ->
PointT point ->
([Linear label point],[[Linear label point]])
visitPoint sd point = case nextSegment point sd of
Nothing -> ([],[])
Just segment -> visitSegment sd point segment
The function visitPoint is used to create all uncontrollable stretches from a point
forward. Given that points themselves are not part of a path and does not aﬀect
any rules directly, the function simply ﬁnds the next segment using nextSegment
==PAGE:139==
6.5 Uncontrollable Stretches
127
and returns the result of visitSegment. There is a special case, where there is
no next segment. This represents a leaf in the search, and as such results in an
empty result, given that the result is created bottom up.
visitSegment :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
StaticData label point train ->
PointT point ->
Segment label point ->
([Linear label point],[[Linear label point]])
visitSegment sd from (LSegment linear) = visitLinear sd
from linear
,→
visitSegment sd from (SSegment switch) = visitSwitch sd
from switch
,→
The function visitSegment is an auxiliary function used to create all uncontrol-
lable stretches from a segment forward. Given that a segment is either linear
or a switch, this function simply pattern matches the segment and invokes the
corresponding visit function.
visitLinear :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
StaticData label point train ->
PointT point ->
Linear label point ->
([Linear label point],[[Linear label point]])
visitLinear sd from linear =
case filterLinearSD linear sd of
False -> ([],[])
True ->
let to = getOppositePoint linear from in
let (cur,rest) = visitPoint sd to in
case hasSignal linear to sd of
True -> ([linear], cur:rest)
False -> (linear:cur,rest)
The function visitLinear is used to create all uncontrollable stretches from a
linear segment, besides the segment it receives the point preceding point. This
functions has 3 branches. Initially the function ﬁlterLinearSD is invoked on
the linear segment, if the result is False then there exists no path from this
==PAGE:140==
128
Implementation
linear segment to the destination of the current train. As such an empty result
is immediately returned as this represents a leaf in the search, this is used to
detect rule 4. If the linear segment is not ﬁltered out, then the next point is
extracted and visitPoint is invoked. All uncontrollable stretches from the next
point are now known. The function hasSignal is invoked to check if the linear
segment and next point forms a signal. If they do, then the current stretch
returned from visitPoint is added to the list of closed stretches, a new current
segment is now started forming only the currect linear segment, this represents
rule 2. If no signal was present then the current stretch is not closed and the
current linear segment is added to it, this represents rule 1.
visitSwitch :: (Label label, Point point, Train train) =>
StaticData label point train ->
PointT point ->
Switch label point ->
([Linear label point],[[Linear label point]])
visitSwitch sd from switch
| isStemPoint switch from =
let (curD,restD) = visitPoint sd $ downPoint switch in
let (curU,restU) = visitPoint sd $ upPoint switch in
case singleNonEmpty curD curU of
Just cur -> (cur, restD ++ restU)
Nothing -> ([], curD : curU : restD ++ restU)
| otherwise = visitPoint sd $ stemPoint switch
The function visitSwitch is used to create all uncontrollable stretches from a
junction, besides the junction it receives the preceding point. This function has
several paths used to represent diﬀerent rules. Initially a check is performed if
the preceding point was the stem point. If it was the stem point then visitPoint
is invoked for the up and down points, resulting in two results. The function
singleNonEmpty is used to ﬁnd if one of the two current uncontrollable stretches
are empty. An empty stretch implicitly means that the path has been ﬁltered
out in visitLinear. As such if one of the paths are empty, then the non-empty
current path becomes the current path. The two list of closed stretches are com-
bined and becomes the current list of closed stretches, this branch represents
rule 4. If there was no empty current path, then rule 5 is encountered, as such
the two current stretches and closed stretches are all combined into the next
closed stretches, the current stretches being empty as the junction itself is not
part of a stretch. Finally if the preceding point was not stem, then the result is
visitPoint invoked on the stem point, this represents rule 3.
==PAGE:141==
6.5 Uncontrollable Stretches
129
The ﬁnal result is collected into a lookup structure on the form:
type TrainStreches label point train =
Map.HashMap train (Map.HashMap (Linear label point)
(Set.HashSet (Linear label point)))
,→
Which is a mapping from trains to: a mapping of linear segments to: a set
of linear segments. The outer map is used to lookup the stretches relevant to
the trains. The inner map represents a mapping between the entrance to the
segment and the entire stretch. The stretch itself is represented as a set for the
sake of eﬃcient lookup, and the ordering becomes irrelevant once the entrance
has been extracted. In order to create this structure two additional functions
are needed:
uncontrollableStrechesTrain :: (Label label, Point point,
Train train) =>
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
train ->
[(Linear label point,LinearSet label point)]
uncontrollableStrechesTrain rail game apsp train =
let (connections,dirAPSP,start,from) = getDirInfo rail
game apsp train in
,→
let sd = StaticData {
railway = rail,
game = game,
apsp = dirAPSP,
train = train,
connections = connections
} in
let (cur,rest) = visitLinear sd from start in
fmap createEntranceTuple $ filter (not . null) (cur:rest)
The function uncontrollableStrechesTrain receives the RailwayDeﬁnition, GameDef-
inition and APSP information. It also receives the train for which the uncon-
trollable stretches are calculated. Initially it extracts the relevant information
from the train. It extracts the connection map relevant for the train direction,
==PAGE:142==
130
Implementation
it extracts the APSP information relevant for the train direction and it extracts
the initial position of the train. Based on the known information an instance of
StaticData is created and visitLinear is invoked from the trains start position.
The the ﬁnal current stretch is closed, and all stretches are combined into a tu-
ple consisting of the head of the stretch, representing the entrance, and a set of
linear segments, which is the entire stretch in set form. The ﬁnal result is a list
of these tuples, representing an instance of the inner mapping of TrainStreches.
allUncontrolledStreches :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
RailwayDefinition label point ->
GameDefinition label point train ->
APSP label point ->
TrainStreches label point train
allUncontrolledStreches rail game apsp =
Map.fromList $ fmap tFunc $ trains game
where
tFunc t = (t, Map.fromList $ uncontrollableStrechesTrain
rail game apsp t)
,→
The ﬁnal function allUncontrollableStretches is used to calculate the entire
TrainStreches structure. This function simply invokes the function uncontrol-
lableStrechesTrain for all trains, and maps the result to the train itself. All
of the results are then collected into the nested map structure represented by
TrainStreches.
Assuming the time complexity of insertion into a HAMT is O(1), then the time
complexity of constructing TrainStreches becomes O(|R| · |T|). Each element
of the railway network is worst case visited twice during the construction, once
when initially visited, and once when the results are collected. This is done for
each train. The resulting stretches for a train will worst case be of size O(|L|),
and the worst case number of stretches is also O(|L|). The head of each stretch
is extracted, which is constant, and the entire stretch is combined into a set
which has linear complexity. As such the construction of the inner map for each
train is still O(|R|). This is performed for each train, and the construction of
the outer map is linear in the number of trains, meaning the ﬁnal worst case
time complexity become O(|R| · |T|) and worst case space used is O(|L| · |T|).
==PAGE:143==
6.5 Uncontrollable Stretches
131
6.5.2
Prioritisation & Filtration
The operations for prioritising or ﬁltering potential successor states, based on
TrainStreches is very similar.
trainInSetImproved :: (Label label, Point point, Train
train) =>
,→
Set.HashSet (Linear label point) ->
GState label point train ->
train ->
Maybe train
trainInSetImproved set state train =
let tList = Map.toList $ trainPositions state in
let tListM = fmap (\(t,l) ->
(t,forceGamePositionToPosition l)) tList in
,→
let mTrain = listToMaybe $ filter (\(t,l) -> (t /= train)
&& (Set.member l set)) $ tListM in
,→
fmap fst mTrain
The function trainInSetImproved receives a set, a game state and a train. The
function then proceeds to iterate over all trains and their related positions,
found in the state. The train parameter is ﬁltered out as it represents the cur-
rent train. The result of the function is the ﬁrst train found, which is not equal
to the input train, and whose position is inside the set. If no such train exists
the result is Nothing.
==PAGE:144==
132
Implementation
destInOccupiedStretchBothDir :: (Label label, Point point,
Train train) =>
,→
TrainStreches label point train ->
GState label point train ->
Linear label point ->
train ->
Bool
destInOccupiedStretchBothDir stretches state linear train =
let sMap = fromMaybe Map.empty $ Map.lookup train
stretches in
,→
let sSet = fromMaybe Set.empty $ Map.lookup linear sMap
in
,→
isJust $ trainInSetImproved sSet state train
The function destInOccupiedStretchBothDir receives TrainStreches, a GState,
Linear and a train. It looks up the train in TrainStreches, and then looks up
the linear segment in the inner map, resulting in a set of Linear’s. The function
trainInSetImproved is invoked with the set, game state and train. Finally the
Maybe result is transformed into a Boolean. If a train was found the result is
True, otherwise the result is False.
If destInOccupiedStretchBothDir is used as a SubFilter, then a state is ﬁltered
out if the result is True. If the information is used as a Priority, then True will
be prioritised lower than False. This transformation is identical to the transfor-
mation of destInOccupiedChocke described earlier.
The time complexity of the destInOccupiedStretchBothDir function is expected
to be O(|T|). The uncontrolled stretch is represented as a set. An iteration is
made over each train and position pair in the state. A single train is ﬁltered
out, and the remaining positions are looked up in the set. Assuming a time
complexity of O(1) for lookup in a HAMT, the ﬁnal complexity is O(|T|).
6.6
Signalling Plan
In order to create a signalling plan, the ﬁxed-point assignment received from
LiuSmolka is traversed from the initial vertex, following the ﬁrst path found
where all vertices are 1 in the assignment.
==PAGE:145==
6.6 Signalling Plan
133
data SignalState = Red | Green
data SwitchState = Up | Down
type FragPosMap label point train =
Map.HashMap train (Linear label point)
type FragSignalMap label point =
Map.HashMap (Signal label point) SignalState
type FragSwitchMap label point =
Map.HashMap (Switch label point) SwitchState
data PlanFragment label point train = PlanFragment{
fragTrainPositions :: FragPosMap label point train,
fragSignalStates :: FragSignalMap label point,
fragSwitchStates :: FragSwitchMap label point
}
A signalling plan is represented as a list of PlanFragment’s. A PlanFragment is
in essence just a GState where only information regarding train positions, signal
and junction conﬁgurations remain. Rather than deﬁning the signal and junc-
tion conﬁgurations through set membership, a mapping is created to an actual
conﬁguration value.
winningPathFirstTrue :: (Stack (GState label point train)
label point train m) =>
,→
Fixpoint (GState label point train) ->
[GState label point train] ->
m [GState label point train]
winningPathFirstTrue _ [] = return []
winningPathFirstTrue fixpoint (h:t) = do
if (Map.lookup h fixpoint) == (Just True) then do
tNext <- winningPathsSucc fixpoint h
return (h : tNext)
else
winningPathFirstTrue fixpoint t
The function winningPathFirstTrue receives the ﬁxed-point assignment and a
list of states. It then proceeds to ﬁnd the ﬁrst state which is assigned the value
==PAGE:146==
134
Implementation
1 in the ﬁxed point. If no such value is found, the search is complete otherwise
winningPathsSucc is invoked to ﬁnd the next vertex in the path.
winningPathsSucc :: (Stack (GState label point train) label
point train m) =>
,→
Fixpoint (GState label point train) ->
(GState label point train) ->
m [GState label point train]
winningPathsSucc fixpoint state = do
succ <- allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves
(relevantSuccessorStates filterThroughFloydWarshall
prioritizeShortestPath) state
,→
,→
let succFlat = concatMap id succ
winningPathFirstTrue fixpoint succFlat
The function winningPathsSucc receives the ﬁxed point assignment and a state
which is 1 in the assignment. It then proceeds to expand the state using a suc-
cessor function. The resulting successors are ﬂattened into a list and provided
as an input for winningPathFirstTrue.
winningPath :: (Stack (GState label point train) label
point train m) =>
,→
(GState label point train, (Fixpoint (GState label point
train))) ->
,→
m (Maybe [PlanFragment label point train])
winningPath (v0,fixpoint) =
if Map.lookup v0 fixpoint /= Just True then
return Nothing
else do
path <- winningPathsSucc fixpoint v0
let pathFilter = filter (\s -> (latestMoveBy s) ==
Controller) path
,→
plan <- mapM stateToPlanFragment pathFilter
return (Just plan)
The function winningPath is used to create the full signalling plan. It receives
the initial vertex and ﬁxed point assignment. If the initial vertex is 0 in the
assignment the value Nothing is returned, indicating no plan exists. Otherwise
==PAGE:147==
6.7 Combined Solution
135
the function winningPathsSucc is invoked with the assignment and initial vertex.
The result is a list of states. The Conductor states are ﬁltered out, and the
remaining Controller states are turned into PlanFragments. The resulting list
is the correct-by-construction signalling plan.
6.7
Combined Solution
Here the combination of the previous sub-modules are shown, forming the com-
bined solution.
expandAndtransformEdges :: (Stack k label point train m) =>
(GState label point train -> m [GState label point
train]) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m [[GState label point train]]
expandAndtransformEdges succFunc state = do
successors <- succFunc state
case latestMoveBy state of
Controller | null successors -> return []
Controller -> return [successors]
Conductor -> return (fmap return successors)
The function expandAndtransformEdges receives a successor function and turns
it into a hyper-edge function. This is done by invoking the successor function.
If the result is empty, then an empty list is returned, meaning no hyper-edges
are generated. This means that the assignment of the input state is forced to
be 0. Otherwise if the input was a Controller state, all successors form a single
hyper-edge. If the input was a Conductor state, all successors form their own
hyper-edge.
==PAGE:148==
136
Implementation
allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves :: (Stack k label point train
m) =>
,→
(GState label point train -> m [GState label point
train]) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m [[GState label point train]]
allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves succFunc state = do
victor <- stateVictor state
case victor of
Nothing -> expandAndtransformEdges succFunc state
Just Controller -> return [[]]
Just Conductor -> return []
The function allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves receives a successor function and
produces a hyper-edge function. It initially checks the input vertex. If the input
vertex is trivially won by Controller, meaning all train are at their destinations,
then the result is a single hyper-edge with no destination vertices, forcing the
input state to be 1 in the ﬁxed point assignment. If the state is trivially won
by Conductor, meaning either a collision or derailment has occurred, then the
result is no hyper-edges, forcing the state to be 0 in the ﬁxed point assignment.
If the state is not trivially won by any player, then expandAndtransformEdges
is invoked. It is allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves applied with a successor function
which should be provided to liuSmolkaLocalT in order to have the correct ef-
fect. The function allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves is the implementation of the
function allSuccessorsDep described in the architecture.
liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint :: (Stack (GState label point
train) label point train m) =>
,→
(GState label point train -> m [GState label point
train]) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m (GState label point train, (Fixpoint (GState label
point train)))
,→
liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint succFunc state = do
liuSmolkaLocalT (allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves succFunc)
state
,→
The function liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint receives a successor function wherein
ﬁlters and priorities may be embedded, and an initial state. The function then
==PAGE:149==
6.7 Combined Solution
137
invokes liuSmolkaT with the successor function transformed into a hyper edge
function via allSuccessorStatesWithLeaves. The result is a ﬁxed-point assign-
ment of the game.
fullLiuSmolkaGame :: (Stack (GState label point train)
label point train m) =>
,→
(GState label point train -> m [GState label point
train]) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m (Maybe [PlanFragment label point train])
fullLiuSmolkaGame succFunc state = do
liuRes <- liuSmolkaRailwayFixpoint succFunc state
winningPath liuRes
The function fullLiuSmolkaGame receives a successor and an initial state. It
produces and optional signalling plan inside the railway monads.
The func-

6.7.1
Combining ﬁlters and priorities
The module Game.Games contains the default SubFilter’s and Priority’s. These
can be combined in order to create new SubFilter’s and Priority’s. SubFilters
can be transformed into Filter’s.
combine3 comb f1 f2 x y z = do
a <- f1 x y z
b <- f2 x y z
return $ comb a b
createDestFilterM :: (Monad m) =>
(t -> t1 -> t2 -> m Bool) ->
(t -> t1 -> t2 -> m Bool) ->
(t -> t1 -> t2 -> m Bool)
createDestFilterM = combine3 (&&)
createPriorityM :: (Ord a, Ord b, Monad m) =>
(GState label point train -> Linear label point -> train
-> m a) ->
,→
(GState label point train -> Linear label point -> train
-> m b) ->
,→
(GState label point train -> Linear label point -> train
-> m (a,b))

The function combine3 is a generic function receiving two 3-parameters func-
tions which returns a monadic value. The result is a new 3-parameter function
which invokes the two input functions and combines the two outputs with a pro-
vided combination function. The function createDestFilterM is used to combine
two SubFilter’s. This function is deﬁned via combine3 which logical conjunction
as the combination function. The function createPriorityM is used to combine
two Priority’s. The function is deﬁned via combine3, the combination function
receives the two input and combines them into a tuple.
The standard total
ordering relation of Haskell tuples is deﬁned lexicographically, meaning the or-
dering is ﬁrst done according to the ﬁrst element, then according to the second.

combine1 comb f1 f2 x = do
a <- f1 x

The function combine1 is similar to combine3 except it operates on 1-parameter

allSuccessorsValid :: (Railway label point train m) =>
(GState label point train -> train -> Linear label point
-> m Bool) ->
,→
GState label point train ->
m Bool
allSuccessorsValid predicate state = do
succPos <- allTrainDestinations state
posValid <- mapM mapper succPos
return $ all id posValid
where
mapper (_,Derail) = return False
mapper (t,Position linear) = predicate state t linear
The function allSuccessorsValid is used to transform a SubFilter into a Filter.
This is done by invoking allTrainDestinations, which is described in section 6.2.3,
on the input GState. The result is a list of possible train destinations. All of
these destinations are then mapped through the input SubFilter and combined
using conjunction. Because the destinations found via allTrainDestinations is
of type GamePosition rather than Linear an extra condition is added to the
SubFilter. If any destination is Derail the result is False.

6.8
Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
One of the reasons for choosing Haskell for the implementation, is because of
its implicit non-strict semantics. One of the goals is to explore how these se-
mantics could serve to make the translation of an abstract algorithm into an
implementation more direct as the details regarding evaluation are abstracted
away by the language. In order to answer this question some introduction to
Haskell’s semantics is required.
6.8.1
Evaluation Strategy & Normal Forms
The main novelty of Haskell’s semantics is that they are non-strict as opposed
the the strict semantics which is the norm for most mainstream programming
languages. Strict semantics usually means that a value is always evaluated in-
side out, which is to say in the expression 1 + (2 + 3), (2 + 3) is evaluated ﬁrst
followed by the evaluation of 1 + 5. For function application this means that
the parameters are evaluated before the function is evaluated, e.g. in f(2 + 3),
(2 + 3) is evaluated ﬁrst, followed by the evaluation of f(5). If an expression
is represented as an abstract syntax tree (AST), this means that the tree is
evaluated bottom up [Apfb]. The AST for 1 + (2 + 3) can be seen on ﬁgure 6.1a
and the AST for f(2 + 3) ﬁgure 6.1b.
Non-Strict semantics operate opposite to strict semantics, meaning expressions
are evaluated outside in or, in terms of an AST, top down. Meaning 1 + (2 + 3)
results in the outermost + being evaluated before the innermost. For function
==PAGE:153==
6.8 Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
141
application this means that a parameter is not evaluated before the function
call is made. Non-strict semantics in the context of Haskell also means that the
evaluation of a value is deferred until needed. In a strict language 1 + (2 + 3)
would immediately be evaluated to 6 and this value would be used. In Haskell
1 + (2 + 3) would remain as an un-evaluated computation (thunk) until the
value is needed, which usually occurs during equality checks, or a partial equal-
ity checks such as pattern matching.
One of the advantages of evaluating expressions top down, is that it allows ex-
pressions to be evaluated partially if the entire expression is not needed. For
1 + (2 + 3) this does not yield an advantage because the expression needs to be
evaluated fully before the value can really be used. One example where par-
tial evaluation could yield an advantage is tuples. Assume the tuple (1, 3) is
deﬁned, AST can be seen on ﬁgure 6.1c, the values of 1 and 3 represents the
fully evaluated ﬁnal results, but assume that they where computed via expensive
computations. In this case deﬁning the tuple does not result in the evaluation
of either 1 or 3. Assume that a later point the the value of 1 is pattern matched.
This pattern matching forces the expression being evaluated, but only the parts
which is required, meaning 3 can remain un-evaluated.
This observations gives rise to the diﬀerent forms which an expression can have
in Haskell. An expression is always evaluated top down, but each node in the
expression tree can remain un-evaluated unless the value of the node in needed
or the value of a successor node is needed. If the tuple example is used again,
the tuple expression can exist in 5 states of evaluation. In this example _ means
an expression is un-evaluated.
1. _: The entire tuple expression is un-evaluated.
2. (_, _): The top node is evaluated, meaning the tuple structure is evalu-
ated. The two tuple components remain un-evaluated.
3. (1, _): The tuple structure and left component is evaluated.
4. (_, 3): The tuple structure and right component is evaluated.
5. (1, 3): The entire tuple is evaluated.
This example does not take into account, that the tuple components can poten-
tially exist in diﬀerent states of evaluation too.
==PAGE:154==
142
Implementation
Figure 6.2: AST: [1,2,3]
In general there exists 2 normal forms. The ﬁrst is simply the normal form,
which means the entire expression has been evaluated. The other is called weak
head normal form (WHNF) this form means that the topmost construct of an
expression has been evaluated. For 1 + (2 + 3), 6 represents both normal forms.
For (1, 3), (1, 3) is the normal form, (_, _) represents the minimum evaluation
satisfying WHNF as the tuple structure has been evaluated.
A ﬁnal note is that the examples presented in this section assumes no compiler
optimisations. The standard Haskell compiler GHC can perform strictness anal-
ysis, meaning if an expression is determined to be evaluated in all code paths,
then it may be evaluated strictly in order to avoid the overhead of non-strict
evaluation.
6.8.2
Consequences of Non-Strictness
The introduction of non-strict semantics allows code to be written where less
emphasis is placed on where to evaluate a value, as this is implicitly deferred
until needed. There are however other consequences some of which positive and
some negative.
Non-strict semantics allows for easy deﬁnition of inﬁnite data structures. The
classical example of this is inﬁnite lists. This basic Haskell list is deﬁned in the
normal functional manner: data [a] = (:) a [a] | []. This means that a list has two
constructors, one representing the empty list denoted [], the other representing
a cons denoted as a colon. A cons is repented as an element called head, repre-
senting the ﬁrst element of the list and a list of elements called tail, representing
the rest of the list. The list instance [1, 2, 3] is a shorthand for 1 : (2 : (3 : [])),
which is represented by the AST shown on ﬁgure 6.2. If the evaluation rules
presented in section 6.8.1 are followed then it can be inferred that in order to
==PAGE:155==
6.8 Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
143
access the ﬁrst element of the list, only the initial (:) and the value 1 itself needs
to be evaluated. If the second element is accessed then only the two ﬁrst (:)’s
are evaluated as well as the value 2. As such an inﬁnite list can be constructed,
meaning there is no [] tail element, as the list does not need to be fully evaluated.
Another eﬀect of this evaluation strategy is that undeﬁned values can be used,
usually denoted ⊥. The value ⊥can be of any type, but will result in a run-time
error if evaluated. A list [⊥, ⊥, 3] could as such be deﬁned, and as long as the
value of the two ﬁrst elements is not forced to be evaluated, a run-time error
does not occur.
One situation where non-strictness could yield a large beneﬁt, is functions where
some code paths results in some parameters not being needed. A simple example
would be a function ifElse :: Bool −→a −→a −→a. This function represents an
if then else statement, and receives 3 parameters. The ﬁrst is a Boolean and the
2 others are values. If the input Boolean is True then the result is the second
parameter otherwise the result is the third parameter. In this situation there
will never exist a situation where both the second and third parameter needs
to be evaluated for the same function invocation. In a strict language explicit
measures would be needed to prevent both parameters being evaluated in all
cases, such as representing the values as 0 parameter functions. In a non-strict
world is is provided without any syntactic noise.
Non-strict semantics does however carry some downsides. The ﬁrst downside
is a time overhead. If the value 1 + (2 + 3) is deﬁned, rather than calculating
the value immediately, time is spent creating a thunk representing the value.
Once the value is evaluated the thunk needs to be executed as would occur in a
strict language. Although this creates constant overhead, this will never result
in more evaluation steps being taken. Some of the overhead may be removed
via strictness analysis.
The largest potential pitfall non-strictness can create, is a new ﬂavour of memory
leak. Un-evaluated expressions are stored as thunks, representing the underlying
computation. If a new expression is created using an un-evaluated value, then
a new thunk is created referring to the old thunk. An example of this is if a
list of numbers were to be summed. For the list [1, 2, 3..10] and assuming left
associativity, then initially a thunk representing 1 + 2 is created. Then a new
thunk representing (1 + 2) + 3 is created and so on. The resulting value now
has linear memory usage in the size of the list until it is evaluated, rather than
constant memory usage which could easily be achieved. As such it is conceivable
==PAGE:156==
144
Implementation
(a) Hyper-edges: Controller
(b) Hyper-edges: Conductor
Figure 6.3: Hyper-Edge Construction
that a program path is taken, where the value happens to never be evaluated,
but still stored because it cannot be guaranteed to never happen. This could
occur if the value was stored in a list or some other collection which is used
throughout the lifespan of the program. In order to avoid such a buildup of
thunks, Haskell does provide ways to force strict evaluation, this is however
done explicitly for an expression.
6.8.3
Non-Strictness in Implementation
In the actual implementation, the non-strict semantics has primarily been of
use in two scenarios. The ﬁrst is when expanding the game graph. During the
execution of the LiuSmolka algorithm, if a state is encountered whose value in
the current ﬁxed-point estimation is ⊥, then the algorithm proceeds to expand
the game graph. Deferring the expansion ensures that vertices in the graph,
which cannot be reached from the initial state, does not take any resource. The
expansion however could result in a large number of successor states, which may
never be processed before a solution is found. As such it makes sense to defer
the actual evaluation of a state, until it it is required.
The general approach for expanding states is to invoke a successor function on
the base state, which produces a list of successor states. The logic for performing
this deﬁned as part of the railway game. It is however the LiuSmolka algorithm
which chooses when to invoke this logic, and as such it is where it is decided
when the actual successor states are evaluated.
==PAGE:157==
6.8 Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
145
First before providing the successor information to LiuSmolka, the successor list
needs to be transformed into a list of hyper-edges. There are two ways this can
occur as described in section 3.4. Either each successor forms its own hyper-
edge or all successors form a single hyper-edge. Assume an situation where a
state s1 needs to be expanded the expansion will result in two successor states
[s2, s3]. Figure 6.3 shows the two possible ways this can occur, it should be
noted that the ﬁgure represents the logical ﬁnal result but the areas inside the
dotted border are un-evaluated.
Figure 6.3a shows the result if s1 was a Controller state. The logical result
is a single hyper-edge e1, where the source state is s1 which already exists in
an evaluated form. The destination vertices are represented as the list [s1, s2].
In this case the entire structure represents all hyper-edges with s1 as source
without actually evaluating any of the successors. It should be noted that the
thunk can be partially evaluated, it is for example possible to evaluate s2 with-
out evaluating s3 following the rules described in section 6.8.1. This means that
once the LiuSmolka algorithm starts looking at the destination vertices they are
only evaluated as needed. If A(s2) ̸= 1 then the edge is added to D(s2) and s3
remains un-evaluated.
Figure 6.3b shows the result if s1 was a Conductor state. The logical result is
a list of hyper-edges [e1, e2], each edge having s1 as source and a single state as
destination. The state s1 is already evaluated, and the structure is shared by
the two hyper-edges.
During the execution of LiuSmolka the structure W represents collection of
pending hyper-edges. In order to achieve a DFS like search, this is represented
as a stack which in turn is trivially represented as a cons list. Figure 6.4 and
6.5 shows examples of how W could look over time.
Figure 6.4a shows an initial conﬁguration where the hyper-edges [e1, e2, e3] are
pending the entire structure is un-evaluated. Next it is assumed that an itera-
tion of LiuSmolka occurs, resulting in the removal of an edge. Figure 6.4b shows
how the W list is ﬁrst evaluated to WHNF such that it becomes clear that it is
not empty. Figure 6.4c then shows how W is rebound to the tail of the previous
W, representing the removal of e1. At this point the internal structure of e1 is
similar to one of the possibilities shown on ﬁgure 6.3, but the the destination
vertices remain un-evaluated. LiuSmolka will then start evaluating the destina-
tion vertices in order to check their value if A.
==PAGE:158==
146
Implementation
(a) Pending: Initial
(b) Pending: Evaluate Top #1
(c) Pending: Remove Top #1
Figure 6.4: LiuSmolka: Pending Over Time #1
==PAGE:159==
6.8 Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
147
Figure 6.5a shows and example where the state is expanded and the result-
ing hyper-edges added to W.
Before adding the successors, W contains the
hyper-edges [e2, e3] and the new hyper-edges [e3, e5] are added. The result is
an un-evaluated ++ which represents list concatenation. Each of the sub-lists
are also un-evaluated.
Figure 6.5b shows an example similar to 6.4b where the top needs to be removed
again. Initially the topmost structure is evaluated, which is a list cons. This is
achieved because of the equivalence ([e4, e5]++[e2, e3]) = (e4 : ([e5]++[e2, e3])).
Figure 6.5c again show the rebound W, the remaining hyper-edges still un-
evaluated.
The above description of the lazy evaluation is based on the naive version of
the successor function. In the more advanced versions the concept of ﬁlters and
priorities are introduced, which changes the dynamic of what can be evaluated
in a non-strict manner. When calculating Controller states a priority can be
provided which is used to sort what order the states are expanded in. This does
however conﬂict with the non-strictness. Assume a non-strict list of elements.
Due to the non-strict semantics, sorting the list remains entirely non-strict ini-
tially. Once the head element of the list is removed however more than the
head of the original list needs to be evaluated because the elements needs to be
compared. This is somewhat negated by ﬁrst creating a list of state transitions,
which is sorted. Afterwards the sorted list of state transitions are turned into
full states.
It should be noted that this limitation arise due to the way priorities are imple-
mented. This would still be an issue if the non-strictness was made explicit. As
such there will always be a trade-oﬀbetween diﬀerent solutions and how much
non-strictness can be achieved.
The second important area where non-strictness plays a role is the calculation
if the StaticDeﬁnitions mentioned in chapter 5. The structure will always con-
tain the results of the All-Pairs-Shortest-Path, bottlenecks and uncontrollable
stretches, even if these are never used. Because of the non-strict semantics these
deﬁnitions are evaluated the ﬁrst time they are used, meaning if they are never
used, in a ﬁlter or priority then they will remain as un-evaluated thunks.
The important point of this is that W is treated as a simple list and StaticDef-
initions are treated as a normal record by the implementation. The non-strict
==PAGE:160==
148
Implementation
(a) Pending: Expand Vertex
(b) Pending: Evaluate Top #2
(c) Pending: Remove Top #2
Figure 6.5: LiuSmolka: Pending Over Time #2
==PAGE:161==
6.8 Haskell Non-Strict Semantics
149
semantics shown are inferred based on the evaluation rules of Haskell, meaning
the non-strictness has been achieved with no syntactic overhead. Besides defer-
ring evaluation of states, hyper-edges and static deﬁnitions, non-strictness has
not yielded much beneﬁt in the other parts of the implementation. The memory
leak risks described in section 6.8.2 has not shown to be relevant as there are
no values which accumulates over time, without being evaluated.
Because Haskell is a pure functional language, explicit loop structure are not
present.
One common way to emulate loops is to deﬁne a list and perform
some operation on each element.
Because lists will in general be generated
in a non-strict manner, this results in behaviour comparable to a loop.
As
said this is a very common pattern and is used multiple times throughout the
implementation. These do not deserve special mention as similar results could
be achieved though the use of explicit tail recursive functions.

Chapter 7
Experiments
In this chapter various experiments are run, solving diﬀerent railway games with
diﬀerent optimisations. For the experiments two metrics are recorded, the time
spent solving the problem and how many states were explored by the LiuSmolka
algorithm.
The results are compiled into tables, each table represents the experiments for
a single game. The ﬁrst column is ordered as follows: The ﬁrst line is the rail-
way game, the second line is the ﬁlters used and the third line is the priorities
used. The order of ﬁlters does not impact the results, the order of priorities
may. Table 7.1 shows what ﬁlters and priorities are used. It should be noted
that there is one special case ’naive’ which is the totally un-optimised solution
and as such does use any ﬁlters or priorities. All other examples makes use
of the relevant state successor function. The railway games can be seen in ap-
pendix C, the complexities of the games are also summarised here as: Number
of linear segments, number of junctions, number of signals and number of trains.
Some games were not solvable with certain combinations of ﬁlters and priori-
ties. Here not solvable means that a result was not found within 10 minutes or
the machine ran out of memory. As such some false negative results may be
present, as a result may have been found if more time was allotted. Because

Filter/Priority
Description
ConstTrue
The identity ﬁlter.
This ﬁlter always returns True
meaning nothing is ﬁltered.
ShortestPath
The priority use of the APSP information. The path
with the shortest distance to the intended destination is
prioritised.
FloydWarshall
The ﬁlter use of the APSP information.
States from
where there is no path for a train to its destination are
ﬁltered out.
OverlappingPath
The priority usage of overlapping paths. Destinations
which are inside an occupied overlapping path is de-
prioritised.
Bottleneck
Similar to OverlappingPath but only true bottlenecks
are used. This can be used both as a ﬁlter and priority.
If used as a ﬁlter, then states where a train enters an
occupied bottleneck are ﬁltered out.
UncontrolledBoth
Represents the uncontrollable stretches. If used as a pri-
ority trains are de-prioritised if they are about to enter
an occupied uncontrollable stretch. If used as a ﬁlter
these states are ﬁltered out.

example uses the Florence railway, which is fairly large, and has a large number
of trains. Here only relevant successors and shortest path yield improvements.
This is because all trains moves in the same direction, and there is enough con-
trol over the trains, such that the shortest path priority is able to guide one
train to its destination, before the next train starts moving.
Table 7.11 shows the results of Florence-Link-1, shown in appendix C.10. Here
it can be seen that relevant successors and shortest path are still enough to
reduce the search space to a manageable size. Filtering using APSP yields a
large improvement. This is probably because there is ample opportunity for the
blue train to move in wrong directions, due to the large number of junctions it
needs to pass. Uncontrollable stretches also yields a signiﬁcant improvement,
both when used as a ﬁlter and priority. This is likely because most of the green
and blue trains paths are uncontrollable stretches, meaning if both trains leave
their initial position, then collision cannot be avoided before blue has moved out
of greens path. Using bottlenecks as a ﬁlter also yields signiﬁcant improvement,
this is because the trains needs to move in a very speciﬁc order in order to avoid
collision. The red train needs to wait for green to move, and green needs to
wait for blue to move. Red is stopped immediately because its ﬁrst move would
enter into a bottleneck occupied by green. Green is also stopped fairly quickly
as moving into the fourth row before blue has entered the ﬁfth row, would be
to enter into an occupied bottleneck. In general, combining the diﬀerent ﬁlters
yields an even further reduction is search space, but there are diminishing re-
turns.
Table 7.12 shows the results of Florence-Link-2, shown in appendix C.11. From
now on relevant successors with shortest path is no longer enough to ﬁnd a
solution when multiple trains are moving in opposite directions. In general it
can be seen that the results are somewhat similar to Florence-Link-1. APSP
and uncontrollable stretches yields very good results, for the same reason as
before. The bottleneck used as a ﬁlter also yields very good results, while the
priority approach does not. The Filter approach yields good results because
the movement order between the red, green and blue trains are still ﬁxed. The
other trains also have bottlenecks between them, which is detected. The priority
approach for bottlenecks yields mediocre results, again highlighting the ﬂaws of
this approach.

good results, without any other approach being able to improve it.

because all trains are moving in the same direction and are all under control

becoming unmanageable. Uncontrollable stretches still performs very well. Bot-
tlenecks starts to show problems, only making the problem solvable if used as a
ﬁlter and combined with APSP.
Table 7.16 shows the results of Florence-Double-TwoWay-NoPlan, shown in ap-
pendix C.12.4. This is a version of Florence Double where there exists no win-
ning strategy.
Because only 5 trains are simulated, APSP as a ﬁlter is still
enough to determine that no solution exists. Uncontrollable stretches and bot-
tlenecks as ﬁlters still serves to signiﬁcantly reduce the search space. It can
