however be seen that because no plan exists the search space remains large.
Table 7.17 show the results of Florence-Quad, shown in appendix C.13. This is
the largest test game which can be solved, being 4 times the size of Florence
simulating 22 trains. Here only the Uncontrollable stretches is needed to make
the problem solvable, reducing the search space to an extreme degree. This
game also shows the problem related to bottlenecks and APSP. Even though
only 1040 states are explored when using uncontrollable stretches, the running
time of the game is 5.5 seconds. This is likely because APSP is still calculated
in order to ﬁnd the shortest paths. Because this runs in O(|R|3) the size of
the network is starting to have a large eﬀect. The bottleneck approach cannot
ﬁnd a solution, even if used in combination with uncontrollable stretches. This
shows that the construction of bottlenecks is becoming too expensive because
there exists too many possible paths and bottlenecks.
The last game Florence-Quad-2, shown in appendix C.14, was too large to de-
termine if a plan exists or not. The game is identical to Florence-Quad except
an extra train is added. One can intuitively determine that a plan does exist.
The plan would be identical to the one for Florence-Quad. After the pre-existing
trains are guided to their destinations, the new train can be guided to its des-
tination. There are several reason why adding this train makes the game too
large. One aspect is that the destination is not in one of the ends, like the
other trains. This means that the order in which the trains are moved becomes
more important, as the new train may block other paths if it is not withheld.
Another aspect is that the destination does not have a signal. This means that
==PAGE:179==
7.1 Discussion of Optimisations
167
the new train cannot be forced to stop at its destination. Again this means that
it should arrive at its destination last, otherwise Conductor could move it away
from its destination again. The ﬁnal part of the problem arises because the
train is closer to its destination than most, if not all other trains. This means
that the game will prioritise the movement of the new train, despite it being the
worst possible strategy.
7.1
Discussion of Optimisations
Here a discussion is given regarding the diﬀerent optimsations. When are they
useful and what possibilities exist for improvement.
In general Florence-Quad has shown that even though the pre-processing steps
run in polynomial time relative to the game size, larger games can become a
problem.
7.1.1
Relevant Controller States
This optimisation has been extremely useful and is applicable in almost all
networks. The main advantage of this approach is that in general only a small
subset of signals and junctions, are relevant to conﬁgure at any given time. As
such this approach serves negate the increase in search space, when more signals
and junctions are added to the network.
7.1.2
All-Pairs-Shortest-Path
The APSP information has proven to be useful both when used as a ﬁlter and
as a priority. The ﬁlter version serves to remove parts of the state space where
trains are following paths which will never lead to a valid destination. These
invalid paths are a result of adding junctions to the system. As such the ﬁlter
version serves to negate the increase in state space which would otherwise occur
by adding junctions, which does not create a path to a valid destination.
Using APSP as a priority serves as a way to prioritise the shortest paths to
==PAGE:180==
168
Experiments
goal. The result is that an attempt is made to guide the train, which is closest
to its destination, before any other train is allowed to move. This can reduce
the number of collision events which are encountered, and serves to simulate
potential optimal paths ﬁrst. This simulating the shortest path ﬁrst may not
reduce the percentage of the game graph explored for some games. An example
is Florence-Link-1, here the optimal approach would be to simulate the longest
path ﬁrst, and the shortest path last. Given however that the alternative is a
random prioritisation, this is not expected to yield a worse result in the general
case.
Given that APSP has proven so useful it could make sense to explore the possi-
bility of using a faster algorithm to solve the problem. This of course depends on
how large networks are expected to be solved. Calculating APSP for Florence-
Quad took around 5 seconds. If the network becomes larger, them the cubic
complexity starts playing even more of a role. It should also be noted that 5
seconds was achieved on a very fast, at the time of writing, desktop PC.
7.1.3
Bottleneck
The bottleneck approach has proven useful in detecting and removing states
related to collision events. If the relevant successor and APSP information are
used to reduce the search space related to additional signals and junctions, then
collision events are the most common source of increased search space. As such
this approach will in general yield a signiﬁcant reduction in state space. This
approach only yields a beneﬁt when trains moving in opposite directions share
paths. It also was a poor worst case time complexity for construction, meaning
larger games cannot be processed.
It could make sense to further explore how to improve this construction process.
Ideally a better algorithm is found with better worst case complexities.
A
more immediate solution could be to perform this processing on the ﬂy. At the
moment all pre-processing steps are performed in full in the beginning. It may
very well be the case that not all bottlenecks are needed during a simulations. As
such an approach could be taken where the bottlenecks are calculated as needed.
This would likely create some overhead related to checking if a bottleneck is
already calculated.
==PAGE:181==
7.1 Discussion of Optimisations
169
7.1.4
Uncontrollable Stretches
The uncontrollable stretches has in general shown a signiﬁcant improvement in
the explored search space. This approach serves to detecting and removing col-
lision events. As such there is some overlap with the bottleneck approach. This
approach however has some diﬀerent characteristics, which could be advanta-
geous in some circumstances and disadvantageous in other.
The ﬁrst clear advantage is the calculation cost, which is linear relative to the
number of trains and the network size. As such this approach can be used for
much larger games, before the calculation cost becomes signiﬁcant. One point of
diﬀerence is, that this approach uses uncontrollable stretches. As such the use-
fulness is directly correlated to how many uncontrollable stretches are present,
which is larger than 1, and how large these are. As such if a railway network was
created, where all possible signals are added, then this approach would become
useless. One potential advantage is that this approach can avoid collision events
between trains moving in the same direction. This is however already somewhat
negated by shortest path priorty.
As such the conclusion is that uncontrollable stretches and bottlenecks serve to
avoid roughly the same scenarios. Uncontrollable stretches is however signiﬁ-
cantly faster and as such is useful for larger networks.
7.1.5
Combinations
There exists many ways in which the described ﬁlters and priorities can be
combined. Some synergies well, while others do not. In general it can be seen
that bottlenecks and uncontrollable stretches does not synergies well, as they
serve to detect roughly the same scenarios. Relevant successors is very useful in
almost all situations, APSP in both ﬁlter and priority forms are useful in most
situations. As such the ideal combination is relevant successors, with APSP
ﬁlter plus either uncontrollable stretches or bottleneck ﬁlter. APSP should also
be used as a priority in order to prioritise the shortest paths.
==PAGE:182==
170
Experiments
==PAGE:183==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
This project has explored the feasibility of constructing correct-by-construction
signalling plans via game-based methods. This was done by modelling a railway
network from which a reachability game is constructed and solved through the
use of the LiuSmolka algorithm.
The main problem faced when using this approach is the exponential growth of
game states relative to the number of signals, junctions and trains. The LiuS-
molka algorithm runs in linear time relative to the game size and as such is quite
eﬃcient, but the state space is exponential quickly making games unfeasible to
solve.
In order to reduce the eﬀective size of the game which needs to be explored,
several steps have been taken. The non-strict semantics of Haskell has proven
useful in deferring evaluation steps of the LiuSmolka algorithm, until they are
deﬁnitely needed, without introducing syntactic noise.
A technique is introduced for identifying the minimum set of Controller states,
which still guarantees that all Conductor vertices are explored.
==PAGE:184==
172
Conclusion
The Floyd-Warshall algorithm has been used to solve the All-Pairs-Shortest-
Path problem on the railway model. The result is information which is used to
prioritise the shortest paths when possible, and ﬁlter out game states in which
there exists no path for a train to reach its destination.
The concept of uncontrollable stretches was introduced and represents paths
through the railway network, where the Controller player cannot inﬂuence a
train.
This information has proven very useful in ﬁltering out game states
where two trains are inside an uncontrollable stretch simultaneously. Because
of the game rules, Controller cannot prevent a collision between trains in these
regions making the states safe to ﬁlter out.
The concept of overlapping paths has also been introduced. An overlapping path
is a stretch of railway in which two trains share a possible path. Based on this
knowledge, the idea was that states allowing two train to enter an overlapping
path would be de-prioritised because the probability of collision is enlarged.
This concept had limited success. The overlapping paths was later reﬁned as
true bottlenecks by constructing a small reachability game for the two trains
in question. The game was solved in a manner similar to the main reachability
game. If no winning strategy existed for this small game, then the overlapping
path was a true bottleneck. Having identiﬁed true bottlenecks, states allowing
trains to enter bottlenecks simultaneously could be ﬁltered, yielding improved
results.
The identiﬁed ﬁlters and priorities are composable making it possible to com-
bine these for greater eﬀect. The technique for creating the minimum set of of
Controller states together with the APSP ﬁlter combined with a either bottle-
neck the ﬁlter or uncontrollable stretches ﬁlter, and the shortest path priority
has proven to synergise well. The bottleneck ﬁlter and uncontrollable stretches
ﬁlter does not mix well because there is a large overlap in the states they ﬁlter,
namely states with collision events, resulting in diminishing returns.
In conclusion it is quite possible to model a railway game as a dependency graph,
and ﬁnd the ﬁxed-point assignment of this graph in linear time, using a general
algorithm. The game graph will however grow exponentially in size. Several
attributes can be detected in the railway network, which allows for the reduc-
tion of the game graph. These attributes can be found during pre-processing
and runs in polynomial time relative to the railway network. These attributes
contribute greatly in reducing the fraction of the game graph which is explored,
making it possible to solve fairly large problem instances in less than a minute.
==PAGE:185==
173
Given the usefulness of the pre-processing techniques further work could be done
in this ﬁeld. The Floyd-Warshall algorithm and bottleneck construction both
run in polynomial time relative to the railway network but the polynomial factor
is large enough that larger networks becomes costly to process, especially for the
bottleneck construction. As such further work could be done, identifying more
eﬃcient algorithms for solving All-Pairs-Shortest-Path, and calculating the bot-
tlenecks on demand.
It could also make sense to try ﬁnd techniques which makes problems like
Florence-Quad-2 solvable by identifying other attributes of a railway game.
Another aspect which could be interesting would be to utilise the priority con-
cept, together with a meta-heuristic approach in order to discover new eﬃcient
ways to guide the search.
==PAGE:186==
174
Conclusion
==PAGE:187==
Appendix A
Building Solution
The solution is built using the Haskell programming language, using the ’Haskell
Stack Tool Stack’ https://docs.haskellstack.org/. In order to build the so-
lution a valid installation of Haskell Stack is needed. The solution is built using
version 1.7.1, using a never version should not cause problems, but this is the
supported version. GHC version 8.2.2, is used to compile the code, again never
version should be compatible but this is the canonical version.
In order to build the project. Extract the archived folder to some destination
where the user has execution rights. Open a command line and navigate such
that the current directory is ’<SomeLocation>/RailwayHaskell’. From here run
the command ’stack build’. This action should download the correct version of
GHC, download all dependencies and build an executable ﬁle. This ﬁle can be
executed using the command ’stack exec RailwayHaskell-EXE’, this ﬁle does
however not have a good main function and as such this is not encouraged.
==PAGE:188==
176
Building Solution
==PAGE:189==
Appendix B
Running Solution
In order to run the implementation the function testLiuSmolkaFullGamePlan
in the namespace CombinedGameTest needs to be invoked with: A successor
function, a RailwayDefinition, a GameDefinition and the initial GState.
The result is an optional list of plan fragments representing the correct-by-
construction signalling plan if a plan was found.
This can be achieved by either creating a new Haskell project which includes
the implementation. Compile and run it. Alternatively open a command line in
the directory ’<SomeLocation>/RailwayHaskell’ and run the command ’stack
ghci’.
This will start a Haskell REPL environment initialised with the im-
plementation. This is easier to achieve, but the code will be run interpreted
meaning a noticeable slowdown may be experienced. Note that the result of
testLiuSmolkaFullGamePlan can be printed (which is done implicitly if run
in GHCI). The result is printed in a JSON format.
An example invocation is:
==PAGE:190==
178
Running Solution
testLiuSmolkaFullGamePlan
(relevantSuccessorStates
(allSuccessorsValid
(createDestFilterM
filterThroughFloydWarshall
filterThroughUncontrolledStretchesBoth))
prioritizeShortestPath)
dslLyngbyRail1
dslLyngbyGame1
dslLyngbyState1
This will solve the Lyngby-DSL1 game. The relevant successor function is used.
States are ﬁrst ﬁltered according to Floyd-Warshall and then by Uncontrollable
stretches. States are prioritised according to shortest path.
This example is the one used to produce the signalling plan sown in appendix D.
Alternatively the command ’stack test’ can be run to run the automatic test
suite. This will provide some information regarding explores states and time
spent for diﬀerent test examples. This is however not recommended as it will
take very long, and contains examples which will result in out of memory errors.

==PAGE:203==
Appendix D
Signal Plan - Lyngby-DSL1
The following is an example of a signalling plan for the Lyngby-DSL1 Game.
The plan is represented as a list of JSON objects. Each object contains ’Train
Positions’ which maps trains onto linear segment labels. ’Switches’ maps all
switch labels onto either ’Up’ or ’Down’, representing the conﬁguration of a
junction. ’Signals’ maps all signals onto either ’Red’ or ’Green’, representing
the conﬁguration of a signal. A signal is represented as a label for a linear seg-
ment, and a points.
The plan can also be seen as a separate ﬁle in the source code, named ’lyn-
gby1.json’.
